The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Definition and Divisions of Qiyas
Definition and classification of syllogism into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical and conditional syllogisms and identify their sub-types in Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Islamic logicians expanded Aristotle's syllogistic by dividing it into categorical and conditional forms.

This passage introduces syllogism (qiyas) as the first and most important inferential method in Islamic logic. The author explains that Islamic logicians went beyond Aristotle by classifying syllogisms into categorical and conditional types, with conditional further subdivided into conjunctive and disjunctive. This lesson will help you identify these divisions in logical works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
What is being defined? The passage defines qiyas (syllogism). Locate where it says 'القياس' and note its context as the first inferential method.
Qiyas in mantiq is strictly syllogism, not analogical reasoning.
2
Locate the genus
Under what broader category does qiyas fall? It is part of 'the second part of assents' (القسم الثاني من التصديقات) and 'topics of inference' (مباحث الإستدلال).
3
Extract the two main divisions
The passage divides qiyas into categorical syllogisms (القياسات الحملية) and conditional syllogisms (القياسات الشرطية). Identify these terms in the Arabic.
This division was an Islamic addition, not found in Aristotle.
4
Subdivide conditional syllogisms
Conditional syllogisms are further divided into conjunctive (اتصالية) and disjunctive (انفصالية). Locate these terms in the Arabic.
Conjunctive: if-then; disjunctive: either-or.
5
Note the historical claim
The author states that Aristotle did not know conditional syllogisms. This highlights the innovation of Islamic logicians.
This historical context is important for understanding the development of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني من التصديقات : هو مباحث الإستدلال ويشمل هذا المبحث عند الإسلاميين الطرق العقلية الآتية : القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل والقسمة الأفلاطونية . الطريق الإستدلالي الأول : هو القياس . وهو الجزء الجوهري الهام من منطق أرسطو ولم يقف الإسلاميون فيه عند مجرد الفكرة الأرسططاليسية - بل أضافوا إليها - متابعة للشراح اليونانيين - مبحثين هامين - أولهما - أنهم قسموا القياس إلى القياسات الحملية والقياسات الشرطية وقسموا القياسات الشرطية إلى اتصالية وانفصالية . ولم يعرف أرسطو القياسات الشرطية بقسميها . ثانيهما - الشكل الرابع الذي أضافه جالينوس ولم يبحث أرسطو فيه أيضاً . ويلاحظ أن هذا الشكل لم يبحث بحثاً وافياً لدى الشراح الأولين الاسلاميين ، ولكن متأخري المناطقة الاسلاميين أفردوا له مباحث طويلة بحيث يضاف إلى ضروبه الخمسة ثلاثة ضروب . يقول الملوي في شرحه على السلم : « وذهب بعض المتأخرين وتبعه كثيرون إلى أن ضروب الرابع المنتجة ثمانية وجعلوا الشرط فيه أحد أمرين ، إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما فالأمر الثاني يقتضي أن ينتج ثلاثة أضرب زائدة على الخمسة السابقة وإن اجتمع في كل من تلك الثلاثة خستان فزادوا ضرباً سادساً وهو جزئية سالبة صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى على نحو : بعض المستيقظ ليس بنائم وكل كاتب مستيقظ ∴ بعض النائم ليس بكاتب وضرباً سابعاً وهو كلية موجبة صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى نحو : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع وبعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بكاتب ∴ بعض متحرك الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع
The second part of assents (taṣdīqāt) is the topics of inference (istidlāl). This topic includes among the Muslims the following rational methods: syllogism (qiyās), induction (istiqrā'), analogy (tamthīl), and Platonic division (al-qisma al-Ifāṭūniyya). The first inferential method is syllogism (qiyās). It is the essential important part of Aristotle's logic. The Muslims did not stop at the mere Aristotelian idea; rather, they added to it—following the Greek commentators—two important topics. First: they divided syllogism into categorical syllogisms (al-qiyāsāt al-ḥamliyya) and conditional syllogisms (al-qiyāsāt al-sharṭiyya), and they divided conditional syllogisms into conjunctive (ittiṣāliyya) and disjunctive (infiṣāliyya). Aristotle did not know conditional syllogisms with their two divisions. Second: the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rābi‘), which Galen added and Aristotle also did not investigate. It is noted that this figure was not fully investigated by the early Islamic commentators, but later Islamic logicians devoted long discussions to it, such that three moods are added to its five moods. Al-Mallawi says in his commentary on al-Sullam: 'Some later scholars, followed by many, held that the productive moods of the fourth figure are eight, and they made the condition one of two things: either the affirmation of both premises together with the universality of the minor, or their difference in quality together with the universality of one of them. The second case requires that three moods be produced in addition to the previous five, and since in each of those three there are two defects, they added a sixth mood, which is a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, as in: Some waking being is not sleeping, and every writer is waking, therefore some sleeping being is not a writer. And a seventh mood, which is a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, as in: Every writer is moving his fingers, and some still-fingered being is not a writer, therefore some moving-fingered being is not still-fingered.'
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a classification and expansion of syllogistic theory. It begins by introducing the second part of logic concerning assent (taṣdīq), which deals with inference. The author lists four rational methods recognized by Islamic logicians: syllogism, induction, analogy, and Platonic division. The focus then turns to syllogism as the first and most important method. The author explains that Islamic logicians, following Greek commentators, made two major additions to Aristotle's syllogistic. The first addition is the division of syllogisms into categorical (ḥamli) and conditional (sharṭī), and further division of conditional into conjunctive (muttaṣil: if-then) and disjunctive (munfaṣil: either-or). Aristotle did not discuss conditional syllogisms. The second addition is the fourth figure, attributed to Galen, which Aristotle also did not treat. The author notes that early Islamic commentators did not fully explore this figure, but later logicians did, adding three moods to the original five, making eight productive moods in the fourth figure. Al-Mallawi's commentary on al-Sullam specifies the conditions for these additional moods: either both premises are affirmative with a universal minor, or they differ in quality with at least one universal. The second condition yields three extra moods. He then gives two examples: the sixth mood (minor: particular negative, e.g., 'Some waking being is not sleeping'; major: universal affirmative, e.g., 'Every writer is waking'; conclusion: particular negative, 'Therefore some sleeping being is not a writer') and the seventh mood (minor: universal affirmative, e.g., 'Every writer is moving his fingers'; major: particular negative, 'Some still-fingered being is not a writer'; conclusion: particular negative, 'Therefore some moving-fingered being is not still-fingered'). These examples illustrate the fourth figure's additional valid forms. The passage underscores the dynamic development of Islamic logic beyond Aristotle.

Key Terms
القياس
القياسات الحملية
القياسات الشرطية
اتصالية
انفصالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two main divisions of syllogism did Islamic logicians add beyond Aristotle?
A) Categorical and conditional
B) Inductive and analogical
C) Conjunctive and disjunctive
D) First figure and fourth figure

Key Takeaway
Islamic logicians enriched Aristotelian logic by classifying syllogisms into categorical and conditional types, a division unknown to Aristotle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism on a fiqh topic (e.g., intoxicants and prohibition) and then rewrite it as a conditional syllogism (if-then). Explain the difference.
?
Find a real example of a conditional syllogism in a usul al-fiqh text (e.g., from al-Ghazali's Mustasfa). Identify its conjunctive or disjunctive form.

The Syllogism
advanced
7 min
The Fourth Figure and Its Additional Moods
Conditions for eight productive moods of the fourth figure with examples of sixth and seventh moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for the eight productive moods of the fourth figure and construct examples of the sixth and seventh moods.

Core Idea
Later Islamic logicians expanded the fourth figure from five to eight valid moods by allowing premises of differing quality with universality.

After establishing the fourth figure (attributed to Galen), the passage notes that early Islamic commentators did not fully explore it. Later logicians, however, under conditions, recognized three additional moods beyond the classical five. This lesson walks through those conditions and the examples of the sixth and seventh moods, illustrating how specific premise combinations yield valid conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the fourth figure's structure
The fourth figure has the middle term as predicate of the minor premise and subject of the major premise. Familiarize yourself with its standard five moods.
The standard five moods are not listed here, but they are known from earlier lessons.
2
Understand the two conditions for additional moods
Al-Mallawi states that the condition for eight moods is one of two: either both premises affirmative with universal minor, or premises differ in quality with at least one universal. Focus on the second condition.
The first condition yields no extra moods beyond the five? Actually, the text implies the second condition gives three extra.
3
Analyze the sixth mood
Minor: particular negative (جزئية سالبة); Major: universal affirmative (كلية موجبة). Example given: 'Some waking being is not sleeping' and 'Every writer is waking' conclude 'Some sleeping being is not a writer'.
Check that the middle term 'waking' is predicate of minor and subject of major.
4
Analyze the seventh mood
Minor: universal affirmative (كلية موجبة); Major: particular negative (جزئية سالبة). Example: 'Every writer is moving fingers' and 'Some still-fingered being is not a writer' conclude 'Some moving-fingered being is not still-fingered'.
Again verify the figure.
5
Test the conditions
For each mood, confirm that premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and at least one is universal. In the sixth mood, minor is particular negative, major universal affirmative; in the seventh, minor universal affirmative, major particular negative.
Both satisfy the second condition.

Source Text

القسم الثاني من التصديقات : هو مباحث الإستدلال ويشمل هذا المبحث عند الإسلاميين الطرق العقلية الآتية : القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل والقسمة الأفلاطونية . الطريق الإستدلالي الأول : هو القياس . وهو الجزء الجوهري الهام من منطق أرسطو ولم يقف الإسلاميون فيه عند مجرد الفكرة الأرسططاليسية - بل أضافوا إليها - متابعة للشراح اليونانيين - مبحثين هامين - أولهما - أنهم قسموا القياس إلى القياسات الحملية والقياسات الشرطية وقسموا القياسات الشرطية إلى اتصالية وانفصالية . ولم يعرف أرسطو القياسات الشرطية بقسميها . ثانيهما - الشكل الرابع الذي أضافه جالينوس ولم يبحث أرسطو فيه أيضاً . ويلاحظ أن هذا الشكل لم يبحث بحثاً وافياً لدى الشراح الأولين الاسلاميين ، ولكن متأخري المناطقة الاسلاميين أفردوا له مباحث طويلة بحيث يضاف إلى ضروبه الخمسة ثلاثة ضروب . يقول الملوي في شرحه على السلم : « وذهب بعض المتأخرين وتبعه كثيرون إلى أن ضروب الرابع المنتجة ثمانية وجعلوا الشرط فيه أحد أمرين ، إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما فالأمر الثاني يقتضي أن ينتج ثلاثة أضرب زائدة على الخمسة السابقة وإن اجتمع في كل من تلك الثلاثة خستان فزادوا ضرباً سادساً وهو جزئية سالبة صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى على نحو : بعض المستيقظ ليس بنائم وكل كاتب مستيقظ ∴ بعض النائم ليس بكاتب وضرباً سابعاً وهو كلية موجبة صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى نحو : كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع وبعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بكاتب ∴ بعض متحرك الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Fourth Figure: Structure and Eight Productive Moods
Author discusses the fourth figure, its added moods, and refutes traditional objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eight productive moods of the fourth figure of categorical syllogism and state the conditions for their productiveness.

Core Idea
The fourth figure expands logic beyond Aristotle, with eight moods under specific conditions.

The fourth figure of categorical syllogism, attributed to Galen, was not fully explored by Aristotle but was later developed by Islamic logicians. They added three moods to the original five, making eight in total. In this lesson, you will learn the structure of the fourth figure and the eight moods, along with the conditions that determine productiveness.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fourth figure's structure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor premise. This is different from the first three figures.
Memorize the position of the middle term.
2
Learn the original five moods
The original five productive moods are well-known (e.g., Barbara, Celarent, etc.) but applied to the fourth figure. The passage focuses on the added three.
You may refer to standard tables of moods.
3
Grasp the condition for adding three moods
The condition is either: (1) both premises affirmative with a universal minor, OR (2) the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with one of them universal. Under the second condition, three extra moods arise.
This condition is stated in al-Malawi's commentary.
4
Examine examples of the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods
The passage gives concrete examples for each. For the sixth mood: particular negative minor and universal affirmative major. For the seventh: universal affirmative minor and particular negative major. For the eighth: universal negative minor and particular affirmative major.
Check that each example satisfies the condition.
5
Verify productiveness
Each example should yield a necessary conclusion. Test by checking whether the premises force the conclusion under the given condition.
Later logicians accepted these as productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث عند الإسلاميين الطرق العقلية الآتية : القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل والقسمة الأفلاطونية .
الطريق الإستدلالي الأول : هو القياس .
وهو الجزء الجوهري الهام من منطق أرسطو ولم يقف الإسلاميون فيه عند مجرد الفكرة الأرسططاليسية - بل أضافوا إليها - متابعة للشراح اليونانيين - مبحثين هامين - أولهما - أنهم قسموا القياس إلى القياسات الحملية والقياسات الشرطية وقسموا القياسات الشرطية إلى اتصالية وانفصالية . ولم يعرف أرسطو القياسات الشرطية بقسميها . ثانيهما - الشكل الرابع الذي أضافه جالينوس ولم يبحث أرسطو فيه أيضاً . ويلاحظ أن هذا الشكل لم يبحث بحثاً وافياً لدى الشراح الأولين الاسلاميين ، ولكن متأخري المناطقة الاسلاميين أفردوا له مباحث طويلة بحيث يضاف إلى ضروبه الخمسة ثلاثة ضروب . يقول الملوي في شرحه على السلم : « وذهب بعض المتأخرين وتبعه كثيرون إلى أن ضروب الرابع المنتجة ثمانية وجعلوا الشرط فيه أحد أمرين ، إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما فالأمر الثاني يقتضي أن ينتج ثلاثة أضرب زائدة على الخمسة السابقة وإن اجتمع في كل من تلك الثلاثة خستان فزادوا ضرباً سادساً وهو جزئية سالبة صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى على نحو :
بعض المستيقظ ليس بنائم
وكل كاتب مستيقظ
∴ بعض النائم ليس بكاتب
وضرباً سابعاً وهو كلية موجبة صغرى وسالبة جزئية كبرى نحو :
كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع
وبعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بكاتب
∴ بعض متحرك الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع
وضرباً ثامناً وهو الصغرى سالبة كلية وكبرى موجبة جزئية نحو :
لا شيء من المتحرك بساكن
وبعض المتنقل متحرك
∴ بعض الساكن ليس بمتنقل
ويذكر شارح الرسالة « المتقدمون كانوا يحصروا الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل في الخمسة الأول وكان عندهم أن الضروب الثلاثة الأخيرة عقيمة لتحقق الاختلاف فيها أما في الضرب السادس فلصدق نتيجة قولنا : « ليس بعض الحيوان بانسان وكل فرس حيوان وكذبها إذا قلنا في كبرى وكل ناطق حيوان ـ وأما في السابع فلصدق نتيجة قولنا كل انسان ناطق وبعض الفرس ليس بانسان وكذبها إذا قلنا في الكبرى : وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان ـ وأما في الثامن فلصدق نتيجة قولنا ـ لا شيء من الانسان بفرس ـ وبعض الناطق إنسان . وكذبها إذا قلنا في الكبرى ـ وبعض الحيوان انسان . والجواب : أن الاختلاف في هذه الضروب إنما يتم إذا كان القياس مركباً من المقدمات البسيطة فكنا نشترط في انتاجها أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيها إحدى الخاصتين فلا تنتفي من تلك النقوط »(۱) .
خرج الشراح الإسلاميون المتأخرون عن شروط انتاج الشكل الرابع . فمن المعروف عن هذا الشكل أنه لا يجتمع فيه « الخستان » إلا في الضرب الخامس ، ففيه تجتمع الخستان ، فتكون الصغرى موجبة وجزئية والكبرى سالبة كلية :
بعض الحيوان انسان
ولا شيء من الجماد بحيوان
∴ بعض الإنسان ليس بجماد
The [logical] investigation according to the Islamists [includes] the following mental methods: syllogism, induction, analogy, and Platonic division. The first inferential method is syllogism. It is the essential important part of Aristotle's logic, and the Islamists did not stop at mere Aristotelian idea—rather, they added to it, following the Greek commentators, two important investigations. First: they divided syllogism into categorical syllogisms and conditional syllogisms, and divided conditional syllogisms into conjunctive and disjunctive. Aristotle did not know conditional syllogisms with their two divisions. Second: the fourth figure which Galen added, and Aristotle also did not discuss it. It is noted that this figure was not sufficiently investigated by the early Islamic commentators, but later Islamic logicians devoted lengthy investigations to it, such that three moods were added to its five [original] moods. Al-Malawi says in his commentary on al-Sullam: "Some later [logicians], and many followed them, held that the productive moods of the fourth are eight, and they made the condition for it one of two things: affirmation of both premises with the universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with the universality of one of them. The second [condition] entails that three moods are produced in addition to the previous five, even though each of those three has two particularities [khusatan]. So they added a sixth mood: a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, as in: Some awake [person] is not sleeping; Every writer is awake; therefore some sleeping [person] is not a writer. And a seventh mood: a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, as in: Every writer is moving his fingers; Some still-fingered [person] is not a writer; therefore some moving-fingered [person] is not still-fingered. And an eighth mood: a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major, as in: Nothing moving is still; Some moving-about [thing] is moving; therefore some still [thing] is not moving-about." The commentator of the epistle mentions: "The earlier [scholars] restricted the productive moods in this figure to the first five, and they held that the last three moods are infertile due to the occurrence of difference [ikhtilaf] in them. As for the sixth: because the conclusion of our saying 'Some animal is not a human, and every horse is an animal' is true, but it is false if we say in the major [premise] 'And every rational [being] is an animal.' As for the seventh: because the conclusion of our saying 'Every human is rational, and some horse is not human' is true, but it is false if we say in the major 'And some animal is not human.' As for the eighth: because the conclusion of our saying 'Nothing human is a horse, and some rational [being] is human' is true, but it is false if we say in the major 'And some animal is human.' The answer: the difference in these moods only occurs if the syllogism is composed of simple premises; we then stipulate for their productiveness that the negative used in them is one of the two [special] properties, so it is not negated from those points." The later Islamic commentators departed from the conditions of productiveness of the fourth figure. It is well known about this figure that the two particularities [al-khusatan] do not come together except in the fifth mood, for in it the two particularities come together: the minor is affirmative and particular, and the major is negative universal: Some animal is human; Nothing inanimate is an animal; therefore some human is not inanimate.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, its moods, and a debate among Islamic logicians about its productive moods. The author begins by noting that Islamic logicians expanded beyond Aristotle by adding two major investigations: the division of syllogism into categorical and conditional (with conditional further divided into conjunctive and disjunctive), and the fourth figure invented by Galen but absent in Aristotle's work. The focus then shifts to the fourth figure, which early Islamic commentators did not fully explore, but later logicians dedicated extensive studies, adding three new moods to the original five—making eight productive moods in all. The author cites al-Malawi's commentary on al-Sullam, which articulates the conditions for these additional moods: either both premises are affirmative with a universal minor, or the two premises differ in quality with one universal. Under the second condition, three extra moods arise despite each having two particularities (i.e., being particular and negative in some way). The examples given for the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods are categorical syllogisms with terms like 'awake', 'writer', 'sleeping', etc. Then the author presents the objection of earlier logicians who considered these three moods infertile because they produce true conclusions in some instantiations but false in others when the major premise is varied. For example, for the sixth mood, substituting 'every rational being is an animal' for the major yields a false conclusion, showing inconsistency. The answer to this objection is that the difference only appears when the syllogism uses simple premises; if we stipulate that the negative used is one of the two special properties (presumably particularity or negation), then the conclusion does not fail. Finally, the author notes that later Islamic commentators departed from the traditional conditions for productiveness of the fourth figure. Normally, the two particularities (al-khusatan) only meet in the fifth mood (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major), as in the example 'Some animal is human; nothing inanimate is an animal; therefore some human is not inanimate.' The passage thus illustrates the evolution of logical theory among Islamic scholars, showing their willingness to critique and refine earlier doctrines.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Debate on Productivity: Objection and Response
Author discusses the fourth figure, its added moods, and refutes traditional objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the earlier logicians' objection to the three extra moods of the fourth figure and evaluate the later logicians' response.

Core Idea
Earlier logicians rejected three moods as infertile due to inconsistent conclusions; later logicians resolved this by refining the conditions for simple premises.

The addition of three moods to the fourth figure was controversial. Earlier logicians argued that these moods are infertile because substituting different premises yields false conclusions. In this lesson, we deconstruct the objection and the response that saved the eight-mood theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three extra moods
Recall from the previous lesson: the sixth, seventh, and eighth moods. Their forms are: (6) particular negative minor, universal affirmative major; (7) universal affirmative minor, particular negative major; (8) universal negative minor, particular affirmative major.
Each has two particularities (khusatan): particularity and negation.
2
Understand the earlier objection
Earlier logicians claimed these moods are infertile because they produce true conclusions in some instantiations but false in others when the major premise is replaced with a different universal proposition. This shows inconsistency.
The passage gives an example for the sixth mood.
3
Examine the specific counterexample for the sixth mood
For the sixth mood, with premises 'Some animal is not human' and 'Every horse is animal', the conclusion 'Some human is not horse' is true. But if we replace the major with 'Every rational being is animal', the conclusion becomes false. Thus the mood seems invalid.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Analyze the later logicians' response
The response is that the inconsistency only appears when the syllogism uses simple premises. When we stipulate that the negative used is one of the two special properties (khusatan), the conclusion holds. In other words, the extra moods are productive under the condition that the premises are not simple (i.e., they have the required particularity and negation).
This refinement allowed the eight-mood view.
5
Evaluate the response
The response essentially updates the definition of productiveness. It argues that the earlier counterexamples are not valid tests because they use simple premises that do not satisfy the new condition. The debate shows how logical theory is refined through critique.
Many later logicians accepted the eight-mood classification.

الطريق الإستدلالي الأول : هو القياس .
وهو الجزء الجوهري الهام من منطق أرسطو ولم يقف الإسلاميون فيه عند مجرد الفكرة الأرسططاليسية - بل أضافوا إليها - متابعة للشراح اليونانيين - مبحثين هامين - أولهما - أنهم قسموا القياس إلى القياسات الحملية والقياسات الشرطية وقسموا القياسات الشرطية إلى اتصالية وانفصالية . ولم يعرف أرسطو القياسات الشرطية بقسميها . ثانيهما - الشكل الرابع الذي أضافه جالينوس ولم يبحث أرسطو فيه أيضاً . ويلاحظ أن هذا الشكل لم يبحث بحثاً وافياً لدى الشراح الأولين الاسلاميين ، ولكن متأخري المناطقة الاسلاميين أفردوا له مباحث طويلة بحيث يضاف إلى ضروبه الخمسة ثلاثة ضروب . يقول الملوي في شرحه على السلم : « وذهب بعض المتأخرين وتبعه كثيرون إلى أن ضروب الرابع المنتجة ثمانية وجعلوا الشرط فيه أحد أمرين ، إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى أو اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما فالأمر الثاني يقتضي أن ينتج ثلاثة أضرب زائدة على الخمسة السابقة وإن اجتمع في كل من تلك الثلاثة خستان فزادوا ضرباً سادساً وهو جزئية سالبة صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى على نحو :

كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع
وبعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بكاتب
∴ بعض متحرك الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع

The [logical] investigation according to the Islamists [includes] the following mental methods: syllogism, induction, analogy, and Platonic division. The first inferential method is syllogism. It is the essential important part of Aristotle's logic, and the Islamists did not stop at mere Aristotelian idea—rather, they added to it, following the Greek commentators, two important investigations. First: they divided syllogism into categorical syllogisms and conditional syllogisms, and divided conditional syllogisms into conjunctive and disjunctive. Aristotle did not know conditional syllogisms with their two divisions. Second: the fourth figure which Galen added, and Aristotle also did not discuss it. It is noted that this figure was not sufficiently investigated by the early Islamic commentators, but later Islamic logicians devoted lengthy investigations to it, such that three moods were added to its five [original] moods. Al-Malawi says in his commentary on al-Sullam: "Some later [logicians], and many followed them, held that the productive moods of the fourth are eight, and they made the condition for it one of two things: affirmation of both premises with the universality of the minor, or their difference in quality with the universality of one of them. The second [condition] entails that three moods are produced in addition to the previous five, even though each of those three has two particularities [khusatan]. So they added a sixth mood: a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, as in: Some awake [person] is not sleeping; Every writer is awake; therefore some sleeping [person] is not a writer. And a seventh mood: a universal affirmative minor and a particular negative major, as in: Every writer is moving his fingers; Some still-fingered [person] is not a writer; therefore some moving-fingered [person] is not still-fingered. And an eighth mood: a universal negative minor and a particular affirmative major, as in: Nothing moving is still; Some moving-about [thing] is moving; therefore some still [thing] is not moving-about." The commentator of the epistle mentions: "The earlier [scholars] restricted the productive moods in this figure to the first five, and they held that the last three moods are infertile due to the occurrence of difference [ikhtilaf] in them. As for the sixth: because the conclusion of our saying 'Some animal is not a human, and every horse is an animal' is true, but it is false if we say in the major [premise] 'And every rational [being] is an animal.' As for the seventh: because the conclusion of our saying 'Every human is rational, and some horse is not human' is true, but it is false if we say in the major 'And some animal is not human.' As for the eighth: because the conclusion of our saying 'Nothing human is a horse, and some rational [being] is human' is true, but it is false if we say in the major 'And some animal is human.' The answer: the difference in these moods only occurs if the syllogism is composed of simple premises; we then stipulate for their productiveness that the negative used in them is one of the two [special] properties, so it is not negated from those points." The later Islamic commentators departed from the conditions of productiveness of the fourth figure. It is well known about this figure that the two particularities [al-khusatan] do not come together except in the fifth mood, for in it the two particularities come together: the minor is affirmative and particular, and the major is negative universal: Some animal is human; Nothing inanimate is an animal; therefore some human is not inanimate.

The Eighth Mood of the Fourth Figure and Its Defense
Author presents the eighth mood of the fourth figure with its defense against variation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the eighth mood of the fourth figure and defend its validity against the objection of variation by applying the condition of special properties.

Core Idea
The eighth mood is valid when the negative premise is one of the two special properties.

This lesson examines the eighth mood of the fourth figure, which earlier logicians considered sterile because its conclusion could vary when premises were altered. You will learn the precise condition that ensures its productiveness and see how to apply it in defending the mood against counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood structure
The eighth mood has a minor premise that is universal negative (e.g., 'Nothing that moves is stationary') and a major premise that is particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some that move are changing'). The conclusion is particular negative.
The order of terms: middle term = moving, major term = changing, minor term = stationary.
2
Understand the objection
Earlier logicians argued this mood is sterile because changing the major premise (e.g., to 'some animal is human') makes the conclusion false, showing variation (ikhtilāf). This threatens the mood's reliability.
Variation means the syllogism is not always productive; it depends on the content.
3
Learn the condition for productiveness
The answer (jawab) states that variation only occurs when the syllogism uses simple premises. To guarantee productiveness, the negative premise must be one of the two special properties (khāṣṣatayn), i.e., it must be a particular negative or a universal negative that is not convertible.
The term 'special properties' refers to the two types of negative propositions that preserve validity in certain moods.
4
Apply the condition to the example
In the given example, the minor premise 'Nothing that moves is stationary' is a universal negative. Check that it is one of the special properties (it is, because it is a universal negative). Therefore, the conclusion 'Some stationary things are not changing' follows necessarily.
If the negative were not a special property (e.g., a particular negative), the mood might fail.
5
Generalize to similar moods
The same condition applies to the sixth and seventh moods of the fourth figure. When the negative premise is a special property, those moods are also productive. This defense resolves the earlier logicians' objections.
Knowing this condition helps you validate or refute syllogisms in the fourth figure.

ويذكر شارح الرسالة « المتقدمون كانوا يحصروا الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل في الخمسة الأول وكان عندهم أن الضروب الثلاثة الأخيرة عقيمة لتحقق الاختلاف فيها أما في الضرب السادس فلصدق نتيجة قولنا : « ليس بعض الحيوان بانسان وكل فرس حيوان وكذبها إذا قلنا في كبرى وكل ناطق حيوان ـ وأما في السابع فلصدق نتيجة قولنا كل انسان ناطق وبعض الفرس ليس بانسان وكذبها إذا قلنا في الكبرى : وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان ـ وأما في الثامن فلصدق نتيجة قولنا ـ لا شيء من الانسان بفرس ـ وبعض الناطق إنسان . وكذبها إذا قلنا في الكبرى ـ وبعض الحيوان انسان . والجواب : أن الاختلاف في هذه الضروب إنما يتم إذا كان القياس مركباً من المقدمات البسيطة فكنا نشترط في انتاجها أن تكون السالبة المستعملة فيها إحدى الخاصتين فلا تنتفي من تلك النقوط » (۱) .
And an eighth mood: the minor is universal negative and the major is particular affirmative, like: Nothing that moves is stationary; some that move are changing; therefore some stationary things are not changing. And the commentator of the treatise mentions: 'The earlier logicians confined the productive moods in this figure to the first five, and they considered the last three moods sterile due to the existence of variation in them. As for the sixth: because the result is true when we say 'Not every animal is a human and every horse is an animal', but false if we say in the major 'and every rational being is an animal'. As for the seventh: because the result is true when we say 'Every human is rational and some horse is not human', but false if we say in the major 'and some animal is not human'. As for the eighth: because the result is true when we say 'Nothing human is a horse and some rational being is human', but false if we say in the major 'and some animal is human'. The answer: the variation in these moods only occurs if the syllogism is composed of simple premises, and we condition for their productiveness that the negative used in them be one of the two properties (i.e., of the minor or major), so it is not refuted by those criticisms.'
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth mood of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first states the mood: minor premise universal negative, major premise particular affirmative. An example is given: 'Nothing that moves is stationary' (universal negative), 'Some that move are changing' (particular affirmative), therefore 'Some stationary things are not changing' (particular negative). Then the author quotes a commentary from Sharh al-Risala which explains that earlier logicians considered only the first five moods of this figure as productive, and rejected the last three (sixth, seventh, eighth) as sterile because their results could vary. The commentator gives counterexamples for each of the three supposedly sterile moods: for the eighth, when the major premise is changed to 'some animal is human', the conclusion becomes false. The answer (jawab) argues that variation only arises if the syllogism uses simple premises (mugalladāt basīṭa) and the negative premise must be one of the two special properties (al-ḍarabān al-khāṣṣa) to guarantee productiveness. This defense maintains the validity of the eighth mood.

Did Islamic Logicians Invent New Syllogistic Moods?
Author notes that later Islamic logicians added three moods to the fourth figure and questions their origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three added moods in the fourth figure and evaluate the claim that Islamic logicians independently developed them beyond Greek sources.

Core Idea
Islamic logicians innovated beyond Greek logic by adding three moods to the fourth figure.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has a complex history. While Greek logicians like Aristotle and Theophrastus discussed it, later Islamic commentators expanded its productive moods. This passage captures a moment of self-awareness: the author notes that later Islamic logicians added three new moods to the fourth figure and questions whether these were derived from Greek sources or independently invented. Understanding this helps us appreciate the originality of Islamic logical thought.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard productive moods
The fourth figure has five standard moods according to earlier logicians. The author mentions that the 'two negatives' (خستان) only occur together in the fifth mood. Recall that two negatives generally do not produce a conclusion, but the fifth mood is an exception.
The fifth mood given is IEO: minor I, major E, conclusion O.
2
Identify the added moods
The author states that three additional moods were added, in each of which 'two negatives occur together' (اجتمع في كل منها خستان). This means both premises are negative. However, the condition for productiveness is that the premises differ in quality (i.e., one affirmative, one negative) and one is universal. This seems contradictory; parsing this condition is key to understanding the innovation.
The exact logical structure of the added moods is not spelled out, but the condition is given: اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما.
3
Examine the historical question
The author explicitly asks: 'Did the Islamic logicians derive these moods from Greek sources, or did they devise them themselves?' This is a critical historical question about the transmission and originality of logical doctrines.
The author notes that these moods are not found in the Greek heritage available to them.
4
Evaluate the evidence
The lack of Greek precedent suggests either that the Greek works containing these moods were lost, or that Islamic logicians independently discovered them. This passage exemplifies the kind of critical self-reflection that characterizes Islamic logical scholarship.

خرج الشراح الإسلاميون المتأخرون عن شروط انتاج الشكل الرابع . فمن المعروف عن هذا الشكل أنه لا يجتمع فيه « الخستان » إلا في الضرب الخامس ، ففيه تجتمع الخستان ، فتكون الصغرى موجبة وجزئية والكبرى سالبة كلية :
بعض الحيوان انسان
ولا شيء من الجماد بحيوان
∴ بعض الإنسان ليس بجماد
ولكن المتأخرين لم يقفوا عند هذه الضروب فقط ، بل أضافوا إليها ثلاثة أضرب ، إجتمع في كل منها خستان لأن شراط إنتاج تلك الثلاثة اختلافهما بالكيف مع كلية إحداهما ، فنتج عن هذا الضروب التي عرضناها آنفاً . لا نحب أن نتوسع في شرح هذه الضروب ، ولكن هل نستطيع أن نبين المصدر الذي استمد منه الإسلاميون هذه الضروب . إننا لا نجدها في تراث اليونان الذي بين أيدينا . فمن أين اذن استمد الإسلاميون هذه الضروب أو بمعنى آخر هل وضعوها هم(١) ؟
The later Islamic commentators departed from the conditions of productiveness of the fourth figure. It is known of this figure that the two negatives do not occur together except in the fifth mood; in it the two negatives occur together: the minor is affirmative particular and the major is universal negative: Some animal is human; Nothing inorganic is an animal; therefore some human is not inorganic. But the later logicians did not stop at these moods only, but added three moods to them, in each of which two negatives occur together because the condition for productiveness of those three is their difference in quality with universality of one of them, so these result in the moods we have presented above. We do not wish to expand on explaining these moods, but can we show the source from which the Islamic logicians derived these moods? We do not find them in the Greek heritage before us. So from where did the Islamic logicians derive these moods, or in other words, did they themselves devise them?
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage examines the development of the fourth figure by later Islamic logicians. The author begins by noting that earlier conditions of productiveness for the fourth figure were set aside: it was known that the two negatives (خستان) could not occur together except in the fifth mood. An example of the fifth mood is given: minor particular affirmative (some animal is human), major universal negative (nothing inorganic is an animal), conclusion particular negative (some human is not inorganic). Then the author states that later logicians added three more moods to the existing ones (making eight total), in each of which two negatives occur together because the condition for productiveness is that the premises differ in quality while one of them is universal. The author then raises a critical historical question: what is the source of these additional moods? They are not found in Greek logical works. Therefore, the author wonders whether the Islamic logicians independently discovered them. This passage reflects the broader theme of Islamic originality in logic.

Why Place the Minor Premise First?
Author discusses the ordering of premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Islamic and modern European conventions for premise order in categorical syllogisms and explain why placing the minor premise first enhances logical clarity.

Core Idea
Premise order is not arbitrary; minor-first makes the specific-to-general movement obvious.

When constructing a syllogism, should you state the minor premise first or the major? This might seem a trivial question, but the order affects how easily the inference is grasped. In this lesson, we explore the Islamic logical tradition's preference for minor-first, contrast it with modern European approaches, and understand the reasoning behind the choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conventions
Notice that Islamic logicians place the minor premise first, then the major. Aristotle did not care about order, but many modern Europeans put the major first. Some modern scholars prefer the Islamic order.
2
Understand the reason for minor-first
The author states that clarity increases when the minor is first because the mind moves from the specific (minor) to the general (major) to the more general (conclusion). This progression is natural and transparent.
3
Relate to the first figure
The first figure (the clearest) is given as an example. In the first figure, the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor, so placing the minor first aligns with the logical flow.
The example in the text is cut off, but we can infer it would be like: 'All humans are mortal (minor), Socrates is a human (major), therefore Socrates is mortal'? Actually, minor first: 'Socrates is a human; all humans are mortal; therefore Socrates is mortal' – that moves from specific to general.
4
Contrast with the opposite order
If the major is placed first, the mind starts from a general rule and then applies it to a specific case. While valid, it may be less intuitive for those unfamiliar with the subject matter.
The author argues that minor-first is superior for clarity.

أما عن المسألة الأولى ـ فان الإسلاميين تصوروا وضع المقدمة الصغرى أولاً ـ ثم المقدمة الكبرى . وترتيب المقدمات لم تكن له قيمة عند أرسطو ، لكن المحدثين في أوروبا يخالفون هذا الترتيب ، فهم يضعون المقدمة الكبرى أولاً ، غير أن بعض علماء مناهج البحث المحدثين في أوروبا يفضلون وضع المقدمة الصغرى أولاً لأن « اليقين في القياس يظهر بدرجة واضحة إذا ما وضعت المقدمة الصغرى أولاً ثم الكبرى ثم النتيجة » أما سبب الوضوح إذا ما وضعنا المقدمة الصغرى أولاً فهو أن الانتقال يكون من شيء خاص إلى شيء عام ـ ثم من هذا الشيء العام إلى ما هو أعم منه فتكون درجات الاستدلال واضحة كل الوضوح . أي أن ينتقل الإنسان مما هو أخص إلى ما هو متوسط بين الأخص والأعم . ثم ينتقل مما هو متوسط إلى ما هو أعم لأن المتوسط مندرج في الأعم « ويبدو هذا في الضرب الأول » لأن من المفهوم أن هذا هو أكثر الضروب وضوحاً ـ فمثلاً إذا قلنا :
The issue is pending! There remain two issues that must be clarified in the topic of syllogism: first, the issue of placing the minor premise first; second, the issue of begging the question (musādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb). As for the first issue: the Islamic logicians conceived of placing the minor premise first, then the major premise. The order of premises had no value for Aristotle, but modern Europeans differ from this order: they place the major premise first. However, some modern European methodology scholars prefer placing the minor premise first because 'certainty in a syllogism appears clearly when the minor premise is placed first, then the major, then the conclusion.' The reason for clarity when placing the minor premise first is that the transition is from something specific to something general, then from this general thing to something more general, so the degrees of inference are completely clear. That is, one moves from the most specific to what is intermediate between the most specific and the most general, then from the intermediate to the most general, because the intermediate is included under the most general. 'This appears in the first mood' because it is understood that this is the most clear of moods. For example, if we say:

Commentary
This passage addresses a methodological question in the study of syllogism: the order of premises. The author states that there are two issues to clarify: first, placing the minor premise first; second, the problem of petitio principii (musādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb). The author explains that Islamic logicians conventionally place the minor premise first, followed by the major. Aristotle did not consider the order significant, but modern European logicians often reverse it, placing the major first. Nevertheless, some modern European methodology scholars advocate for placing the minor first because it yields greater certainty and clarity. The reason is that the mind moves from the specific (minor) to the general (major) and then to the more general (conclusion), making the steps of inference transparent and orderly. The author notes that this is particularly evident in the first mood (first figure), which is the clearest. The passage breaks off with an example that is not completed in the given text.

Order of Premises in Syllogism: Why Minor First?
Author argues for placing the minor premise first in syllogism for clarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue for the minor-first ordering in a categorical syllogism using the principle of natural progression from particular to general.

Core Idea
Minor first makes the inference evident by moving from specific to general.

In constructing a categorical syllogism, the order of premises may seem trivial. However, classical Islamic logicians and some modern methodologies insist that placing the minor premise first leads to greater clarity. Why? Because the mind moves naturally from the particular instance to the general rule. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how it applies to theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish minor and major premises
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion; the major contains the predicate. In the first figure, the minor is the specific, the major the general.
2
Trace the logical movement
From the specific (e.g., 'this act is intoxicating') to the intermediate universal ('every intoxicant is forbidden'), then to the more general ('this act is forbidden'). The progression is stepwise.
3
Apply to a first-figure syllogism from Kalam
Consider: The world is temporally originated (minor). Every temporally originated thing has a creator (major). Therefore, the world has a creator (conclusion). Notice the natural order: specific world → general created things → even more general 'has a creator'. This clarity is lost if the major comes first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن المسألة الأولى ـ فان الإسلاميين تصوروا وضع المقدمة الصغرى أولاً ـ ثم المقدمة الكبرى . وترتيب المقدمات لم تكن له قيمة عند أرسطو ، لكن المحدثين في أوروبا يخالفون هذا الترتيب ، فهم يضعون المقدمة الكبرى أولاً ، غير أن بعض علماء مناهج البحث المحدثين في أوروبا يفضلون وضع المقدمة الصغرى أولاً لأن « اليقين في القياس يظهر بدرجة واضحة إذا ما وضعت المقدمة الصغرى أولاً ثم الكبرى ثم النتيجة » أما سبب الوضوح إذا ما وضعنا المقدمة الصغرى أولاً فهو أن الانتقال يكون من شيء خاص إلى شيء عام ـ ثم من هذا الشيء العام إلى ما هو أعم منه فتكون درجات الاستدلال واضحة كل الوضوح . أي أن ينتقل الإنسان مما هو أخص إلى ما هو متوسط بين الأخص والأعم . ثم ينتقل مما هو متوسط إلى ما هو أعم لأن المتوسط مندرج في الأعم « ويبدو هذا في الضرب الأول » لأن من المفهوم أن هذا هو أكثر الضروب وضوحاً ـ فمثلاً إذا قلنا : سقراط إنسان وكل إنسان فان ∴ سقراط فان انتقلنا من سقراط الخاص ، إلى إنسان ، وهي أعم من هذا الخاص ومتوسط بين سقراط وفان . ثم ننتقل من إنسان إلى الفاني وهي أعم من إنسان فالانتقال طبيعي تماماً .
As for the first issue: The Islamic scholars conceived placing the minor premise first, then the major premise. The order of premises had no value for Aristotle, but modern scholars in Europe oppose this order; they place the major premise first. However, some modern scholars of research methodologies in Europe prefer placing the minor premise first because 'certainty in the syllogism appears clearly if the minor premise is placed first, then the major, then the conclusion.' The reason for clarity if we place the minor premise first is that the transition is from a particular thing to a general thing, then from this general thing to what is more general, so the degrees of inference are completely clear. That is, a person moves from what is more specific to what is intermediate between the specific and the general, then moves from what is intermediate to what is more general because the intermediate is subsumed under the more general. 'This appears in the first figure,' because it is understood that this is the clearest of the figures. For example, if we say: Socrates is a man, and every man is mortal, therefore Socrates is mortal. We moved from the particular Socrates to 'man', which is more general than this particular and intermediate between Socrates and 'mortal'. Then we move from 'man' to 'the mortal', which is more general than 'man'. So the transition is completely natural.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage examines the order of premises in a syllogism. The author notes that Islamic logicians typically place the minor premise first, then the major. This ordering was unimportant to Aristotle, but modern European scholars differ, some preferring the major first. However, the author argues with cited support that placing the minor first yields greater clarity because the mind moves naturally from the particular to the general, then to the more general. The example of the first figure syllogism illustrates this: from the particular 'Socrates' to the intermediate universal 'man' (the minor term), then to the more universal 'mortal' (the major term). The progression is stepwise and evident.

The Critique of Begging the Question in Syllogism
Author presents syllogism example and the critique of begging the question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the critique of begging the question (musadara) against categorical syllogisms and evaluate whether it applies to an Islamic theological syllogism.

Core Idea
The charge of begging the questions whether syllogisms actually produce new knowledge.

This passage presents the classic syllogism example and then introduces the second problem: musadara 'ala al-matlub. This critique, raised by Sextus Empiricus and later echoed by Muslim critics and John Stuart Mill, claims that a syllogism's conclusion is already hidden in the major premise, so it yields no new information. In this lesson, we will unpack this challenge and see why it matters for Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the syllogism
Identify the three terms: subject (Socrates), predicate (mortal), middle (human). Note the movement from the particular 'Socrates' to the universal 'human' and then to 'mortal'. This is a first-figure, Barbara mood syllogism.
2
Isolate the critique of musadara
The critic claims the major premise 'every human is mortal' already contains the conclusion 'Socrates is mortal' because 'Socrates' is a human. Thus, the syllogism adds no new knowledge—it merely makes explicit what was already implicit.
3
Test with an Islamic syllogism
Replace the terms with an Islamic example: 'Wine (khamr) is an intoxicant, every intoxicant is forbidden (haram), therefore wine is forbidden.' Consider whether someone who accepts the major premise from revelation (e.g., Qur'an) gains new knowledge about wine if they did not previously connect the two premises.
4
Assess the validity of the critique
The critique assumes the major premise already asserts the conclusion for every instance. However, syllogisms can be informative by linking premises that are not automatically combined in the mind. In Islamic legal reasoning (qiyas), analogical extension differs, but categorical syllogisms still serve to make implicit connections explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سقراط إنسان وكل إنسان فان ∴ سقراط فان انتقلنا من سقراط الخاص ، إلى إنسان ، وهي أعم من هذا الخاص ومتوسط بين سقراط وفان . ثم ننتقل من إنسان إلى الفاني وهي أعم من إنسان فالانتقال طبيعي تماماً . المسألة الثانية : مسألة المصادرة على المطلوب . وهي أن تكون النتيجة والمقدمة الكبرى شيئاً واحداً ، فالقياس لا يأتي بشيء جديد على الإطلاق . . هذه المسألة وضعها سكستوس امبريقوس ناقداً بها القياس الأرسططاليسي وعرفها الشراح الإسلاميون . وكان لها أثر كبير في كتابات كثيرين من المسلمين الذين نقدوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي كما كان لها أثر كبير في العصور الحديثة ، فرددها جون استيوارت مل وجعلها أساس نظريته المشهورة التي نقد بها قياس أرسطو .
Socrates is a human, every human is mortal, therefore Socrates is mortal. We moved from the particular Socrates to human, which is more general than this particular and intermediate between Socrates and mortal. Then we move from human to mortal, which is more general than human. So the movement is completely natural. The second issue: the issue of begging the question. That is, the conclusion and the major premise are one and the same, so the syllogism does not produce anything new at all. This issue was raised by Sextus Empiricus as a critique of Aristotelian syllogism, and the Islamic commentators knew it. It had a great impact on the writings of many Muslims who criticized Aristotelian logic, as it had a great impact in modern times, repeated by John Stuart Mill and made it the basis of his famous theory by which he criticized Aristotle's syllogism.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage begins by giving an example of a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure: Socrates is human, every human is mortal, therefore Socrates is mortal. The author notes the movement from particular to universal and then to another universal. Then he introduces the second problem (musadara 'ala al-matlub), which is the charge of begging the question: the conclusion is already contained in the major premise, so the syllogism does not produce new knowledge. The author traces this critique to Sextus Empiricus and notes its influence on Islamic critics of logic and on John Stuart Mill. The passage is a critical overview of an ancient objection against Aristotelian syllogism.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): A logical syllogism consisting of two premises leading to a conclusion. [Note: In later Islamic logic, qiyas often includes analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh, but here it refers to Aristotelian categorical syllogism.]
مصادرة على المطلوب (musadara 'ala al-matlub): Begging the question: the fallacy where the conclusion is assumed in the premises, rendering the syllogism circular. [Note: This term is stable across logical traditions, though its application to syllogism is debated.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is too long and complex.
B) It does not produce new knowledge.
C) It is invalid due to logical form.
D) It requires external verification.

Key Takeaway
The charge of begging the question forces us to examine how syllogisms function in Islamic theology and whether they genuinely extend knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an Islamic syllogism in the first figure (e.g., about the prohibition of riba: 'Riba is an increase in a loan, every increase in a loan is forbidden, therefore riba is forbidden') and explain why a critic might claim it begs the question. Then defend the syllogism as producing new knowledge by showing that the major premise is a general principle from revelation, while the minor premise identifies a specific case, so the conclusion is a new particular ruling.

Three Weaknesses of Qiyas al-Gha'ib and Ibn Rushd's Defense
Author presents three weaknesses of qiyas al-gha'ib and Ibn Rushd's counter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three weaknesses of qiyas al-gha'ib presented by the mutakallimun and explain Ibn Rushd's objection to each.

Core Idea
The critique of qiyas al-gha'ib ultimately challenges the necessity of causation, which Ibn Rushd defends as foundational.

In this passage, the author presents three reasons why the method of qiyas al-gha'ib (analogy from the witnessed to the unseen) fails to produce certainty. The mutakallimun argue that it is invalid due to the denial of causality, the incompatibility of temporal and eternal knowledge, and the inability to yield certainty. However, Ibn Rushd counters that these critiques themselves undermine necessary causal relations and dangerously approach sophistry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target method
Recognize that qiyas al-gha'ib is the analogical reasoning from the witnessed (shahid) world to the unseen (gha'ib) realm, used in kalam to prove divine attributes and actions.
2
Extract the three weaknesses
List each of the three criticisms: (a) jam' bi al-'illa is invalid because Ash'aris deny causality; (b) jam' bi al-haqiqah is invalid because temporal knowledge differs from eternal knowledge; (c) the method as a whole does not yield certainty.
3
Analyze the reasoning behind each weakness
For each weakness, note the premise: (a) no necessary causal connection; (b) different kinds of knowledge cannot share an essence; (c) even the strongest form (by cause) fails.
The denial of causality is primarily rational-theological, not legal.
4
Identify Ibn Rushd's counter-critique
Ibn Rushd rejects the first weakness by insisting that denying necessary connections between cause and effect leads to sophistry. He does not directly address the second and third weaknesses in this passage but focuses on the dangerous implication of denying causality.
5
Assess the meta-critique
Ibn Rushd accuses the mutakallimun of being influenced by Sophists, implying that their critique is self-defeating because it undermines the very rationality needed for argumentation.

ينقد بعض المتقدمين من المتكلمين والمتكلمون المتأخرون ـ هذا الطريق ويبينون ضعفه وعدم إيصاله الى اليقين واليقين غاية العقليات وتتلخص أسباب ضعفه فيما يأتي : أ ـ أن الجمع بالعلة باطل . لأنه لا علة ولا معلول لدى الأشاعرة . والمقصود من إنكار الجمع بالعلة إنكارها من الناحية العقلية ، لأننا رأينا أن الاشاعرة أنفسهم اعترفوا بمبدأ العلية في القياس الأصولي . ولا يقبل ابن رشد هذا النقد الذي يوجه إلى قياس الغائب على الشاهد من ناحية أنه لا صلة ولا ضرورة بين الشرط والمشروط وبين الشيء وعلته ودليله . ثم يرجع القول بهذا كله إلى السوفسطائيين . ويقرر أن المتكلمين وعلى رأسهم إمام الحرمين ـ إذا كانوا ينقدون قياس الغائب على الشاهد على هذا الأساس ، فانهم قد تأثروا في هذا النقد بهؤلاء السوفسطائيين . ولا يضيق ابن رشد بنقد قياس الغائب على الشاهد في ذاته بقدر ما يضيق بنقد العلاقة الضرورية بين العلة والمعلول (٢) . ب ـ أما السبب الثاني لضعف هذا الطريق ، فهو أن الجمع بالحقيقة باطل لأن العلم الحادث يخالف العلم القديم ، فكيف يجتمعان حقيقة وحدا مع اختلافهما ؟ لا يقبل كثير من المتكلمين هذا النقد ، لأنه قد يجمع بين العلم الحادث والعلم القديم العلمية نفسها ولكن إمام الحرمين يردد أن القول بالعلمية قائم على القول بالأحوال ـ والقول بالأحوال باطل (۳) . جـ ـ أما السبب الثالث لضعف هذا الطريق : فهو أنه لا يفيد اليقين ، لأن أقوى أنواع هذا القياس هو الجمع بالعلة ، والجمع بالعلة لا يصل بالقائس (۱) .
Some of the earlier and later mutakallimun criticize this method and show its weakness and its failure to lead to certainty—and certainty is the goal of rational sciences. The reasons for its weakness are summarized as follows: (A) The combination by cause is invalid because there is no cause and no effect according to the Ash'aris. The intention behind denying combination by cause is its denial from the rational perspective, because we saw that the Ash'aris themselves acknowledged the principle of causality in legal analogy (al-qiyās al-uṣūlī). Ibn Rushd does not accept this criticism directed at qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid on the grounds that there is no connection or necessity between condition and conditioned, and between a thing and its cause and its indication. Then he traces this whole position back to the Sophists. He asserts that the mutakallimun, headed by Imam al-Haramayn, if they criticize qiyas al-gha'ib on this basis, then they have been influenced in this criticism by these Sophists. Ibn Rushd is not so much bothered by the criticism of qiyas al-gha'ib per se as by the criticism of the necessary connection between cause and effect. (B) As for the second reason for the weakness of this method, it is that the combination by essence is invalid because temporal knowledge (al-'ilm al-ḥādith) is different from eternal knowledge (al-'ilm al-qadīm). So how can they be combined in essence and definition given their difference? Many mutakallimun do not accept this criticism because temporal knowledge and eternal knowledge may be combined under the same 'knowledge-ness' (al-'ilmiyya). But Imam al-Haramayn repeats that the claim of 'knowledge-ness' is based on the claim of states (al-aḥwāl), and the claim of states is invalid. (C) As for the third reason for the weakness of this method, it is that it does not yield certainty because the strongest type of this analogy is combination by cause, and combination by cause does not lead the reasoner to certainty.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the method of qiyas al-gha'ib (analogy from the witnessed to the unseen). The critique comes from some mutakallimun, both earlier and later, who point out its weakness and its failure to produce certainty—which is the ultimate aim of rational inquiry. The author lists three reasons. First, the method of jam' bi al-'illa is invalid because the Ash'aris, in their theological framework, deny causality (i.e., there is no necessary causal connection between cause and effect). However, the author notes that the Ash'aris themselves do use causality in legal analogy (qiyas al-Usul), so the denial is specifically in the context of rational theology. Ibn Rushd rejects this criticism, arguing that the denial of necessary connections between condition and conditioned, or between a thing and its cause, leads to sophistry (al-sūfisṭā'iyya). He claims that the mutakallimun, especially Imam al-Haramayn, were influenced by the Sophists in this rejection. Ibn Rushd is more concerned with the denial of causality itself than with the specific method of qiyas al-gha'ib. Second, the method of jam' bi al-haqiqah is invalid because temporal knowledge (e.g., human knowledge) is different from eternal knowledge (God's knowledge). How can two essentially different things share the same definition or essence? Some mutakallimun respond that they can share the same genus (al-'ilmiyya, knowledge-ness), but Imam al-Haramayn counters that the concept of 'knowledge-ness' depends on the theory of states (al-aḥwāl), which he considers invalid. Third, even the strongest form of this analogy (jam' bi al-'illa) does not lead to certainty, so the entire method fails to achieve its goal.

Key Terms
الجمع بالعلة
العلة والمعلول
السوفسطائيين
العلم الحادث والعلم القديم
الأحوال

According to the passage, what is Ibn Rushd's main objection to the mutakallimun's critique of qiyas al-gha'ib?
A) He agrees that jam' bi al-'illa is invalid because of the Ash'ari denial of causality.
B) He argues that the critique itself is sophistical because it denies necessary connections.
C) He thinks that qiyas al-gha'ib should be replaced by textual evidence.
D) He believes that jam' bi al-haqiqah is the only valid method.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-gha'ib is criticized on three grounds: denial of causality, incompatibility of essences, and failure to yield certainty; Ibn Rushd responds by defending necessary causal relations against sophistry.

Uncovering the Missing Premise: The Implied Syllogism
Author dissects the idea of inference from a single premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the missing premise in an implied categorical syllogism from a kalam argument.

Core Idea
A single premise cannot produce a conclusion; an implied syllogism hides a second premise.

This lesson focuses on the logical structure behind the claim that a single premise can yield a conclusion. Using the example from the passage—substance and accidents—we will see that the argument actually relies on an unstated major premise, making it an implied syllogism (al-qiyas al-mudmar). By learning to spot missing premises, you will strengthen your ability to evaluate arguments in kalam and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stated premise
Read the example: 'The substance is not free of accidents that have a beginning.' This is the only explicit premise given in the source.
This is a theoretical premise (nazari).
2
Identify the conclusion
The author presents the conclusion: 'What is not free of accidents that have a beginning is originated.' This conclusion is claimed to be necessary (daruri).
The conclusion does not follow from the single premise alone.
3
Determine the missing premise
To make the conclusion valid, we need a major premise: 'Everything that is not free of originated accidents is itself originated.' This premise is implied but not stated.
The missing premise is a universal affirmative (A) statement.
4
Reconstruct the complete syllogism
With both premises: Major: Everything not free of originated accidents is originated. Minor: The substance is not free of originated accidents. Conclusion: The substance is originated. This is a valid first-figure (Barbara) categorical syllogism.
The implied syllogism reduces to a standard form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني : هو « إنتاج المقدمات النتائج »(٢) وهو استخلاص النتيجة من المقدمة بحيث تكون المقدمة ضرورية والنتيجة نظرية . وقد تكون المقدمة نظرية والنتيجة ضرورية ، كقولنا الجوهر لا يخلو من الحوادث التي لها أول . وهذه مقدمة نظرية لا يمكن التوصل إليها إلا بدقيق النظر . والنتيجة أن ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث التي لها أول فهو حادث . وهذه النتيجة ضرورية . وينكر متأخرو المتكلمين أن يكون هذا الطريق من أدلة العقول ويرى إمام الحرمين - من بين المتقدمين - أنه لا حاصل للفصل بين النظري والضروري . أي أن نقد هذا الطريق يتصل عنده بمذهبه العام في العلم ، الذي يقرر أن العلوم كلها ضرورية ، وأن النظر ليس إلا تردداً بين أنحاء الضروريات(٣) . ولكن كيف يمكن الإنتاج من مقدمة واحدة ؟ ففي المثال السالف الذكر لا نستطيع مطلقاً أن نصل إلى النتيجة التي وصلنا إليها من مجرد وجود المقدمة الأولى . معنى هذا أننا حذفنا إحدى المقدمات ، إما إعتماداً على ذكاء المخاطب وإما للمغالطة ، ونكون أرجعنا هذا الطريق على هذا الاساس إلى القياس المضمر . لم يذكر المسلمون هذا . ولكنهم اعتقدوا أنه يمكن الاستدلال بمقدمة واحدة . وسيردد ابن تيمية هذه الفكرة - فيما بعد - عن نظار المسلمين أنفسهم .
The second method is 'producing conclusions from premises.' It is extracting the conclusion from the premise such that the premise is necessary and the conclusion theoretical. Or the premise may be theoretical and the conclusion necessary, as when we say: The substance is not free of accidents that have a beginning. This is a theoretical premise that cannot be arrived at except by precise reasoning. The conclusion: What is not free of accidents that have a beginning is originated. This conclusion is necessary. Later theologians deny that this method is among the proofs of the intellect, and Imam al-Haramayn—among the early ones—holds that there is no benefit in distinguishing between the theoretical and the necessary. That is, his critique of this method is connected to his general doctrine of knowledge, which asserts that all knowledge is necessary, and that reasoning is nothing but oscillation between types of necessities. But how can production be from a single premise? In the aforementioned example, we absolutely cannot reach the conclusion we reached from merely having the first premise. This means we have omitted one of the premises, either relying on the intelligence of the addressee or for the purpose of fallacy, and we have reduced this method on this basis to the implied syllogism. Muslims did not mention this. But they believed that it is possible to infer from a single premise. Ibn Taymiyya will later repeat this idea—that it comes from the Muslim theorists themselves.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage discusses the second method of rational proof: deriving conclusions from premises (إنتاج المقدمات النتائج). The author explains that either the premise is necessary and the conclusion theoretical, or the premise is theoretical and the conclusion necessary. An example is given: 'The substance is not free of accidents that have a beginning' (theoretical premise) leads to the necessary conclusion that 'what is not free of such accidents is originated.' This example is a categorical syllogism missing its major premise; it only works if the major premise (e.g., 'Everything that is not free of originated accidents is itself originated') is implicitly assumed. Later theologians (متأخرو المتكلمين) deny that this method is a valid rational proof. Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) argues that there is no real distinction between theoretical and necessary knowledge; all knowledge is necessary for him, and reasoning is only a 'oscillation' among necessities. The author then points out a problem: how can a single premise produce a conclusion? The example shows that without an additional premise, the conclusion does not follow. The missing premise is either assumed (relying on the addressee's intelligence) or omitted deceptively. Thus, this method reduces to an implied syllogism (القياس المضمر). Muslim scholars did not explicitly state this reduction, but they believed inference from a single premise is possible. Ibn Taymiyya will later critique this idea, tracing it back to Muslim theorists themselves.

Key Terms
إنتاج المقدمات النتائج
القياس المضمر
ضروري
نظري

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the example 'الجوهر لا يخلو من الحوادث' cannot produce the conclusion from a single premise alone?
A) Because the premise is false.
B) Because the conclusion is not actually necessary.
C) Because a second premise (the major premise) is omitted, making it an implied syllogism.
D) Because the reasoning is inductive, not deductive.

Key Takeaway
Any argument that appears to derive a conclusion from a single premise actually relies on an unstated premise, reducing it to an implied syllogism.

?
Reconstruct the full categorical syllogism from the following kalam argument: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah because it is uncreated.' Identify the missing premise.
?

Al-Juwayni's Critique of the Necessary-Theoretical Distinction
Author presents al-Juwayni's epistemological critique of the second method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Imam al-Haramayn's argument that all knowledge is necessary and critique the distinction between necessary and theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
For al-Juwayni, reasoning is just oscillation among necessary truths, not a move from necessary to theoretical.

In this lesson, we delve into the epistemological dimension of the passage. Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) rejects the very distinction between necessary (daruri) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge. He argues that all knowledge is necessary and that what we call 'reasoning' is merely a transition between different necessary truths. This radical view undercuts the second method of proof, which relies on that distinction. Understanding his critique helps us appreciate the diversity of epistemological positions within classical Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard view being critiqued
The second method assumes that premises can be necessary (daruri) and conclusions theoretical (nazari), or vice versa. This distinction is foundational for the method.
This view is common among logicians and theologians.
2
State al-Juwayni's objection
Al-Juwayni holds that there is no benefit (la hasil) in distinguishing between theoretical and necessary. He claims that all knowledge is necessary.
This is a radical epistemological claim.
3
Analyze his reasoning
He argues that reasoning (nazar) is nothing but 'oscillation between types of necessities' (taraddud bayna anwa' al-daruriyyat). In other words, every step of reasoning relies on already necessary truths.
This collapses the hierarchy of knowledge.
4
Evaluate the implication for the second method
If all knowledge is necessary, then the premise and conclusion of the example are both necessary. The second method loses its distinctive character; it is simply a necessary inference.
This critique aligns with al-Juwayni's broader epistemological system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Reasoning from Agreed-Upon to Disputed: Scope and Limits
Author describes and critiques the third method of reasoning: analogy from agreed-upon to disputed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of reasoning from agreed-upon to disputed and explain why it is rejected in intellectual matters while accepted in legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Consensus does not make an argument certain in intellectual matters.

This passage introduces the third method of reasoning: using a matter on which there is agreement to prove a disputed matter. The author explains that this method is essentially analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen, common in kalām and fiqh. However, he notes that later thinkers reject it for intellectual (demonstrative) matters because knowledge must be certain, and agreement alone does not guarantee truth. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the right method in the right science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method and its definition
Find the phrase 'al-istidlāl bil-muttafaq ʿalayhi ʿalā al-mukhtalaf fīhi' in the Arabic. Understand that it means using a proposition that disputants already agree on to prove one that is disputed.
Imām al-Ḥaramayn defines it as 'attaching the disputed to the agreed-upon' (ilḥāq al-mukhtalaf bil-mujtamaʿ).
2
Analyze the example given
Author gives an example: comparing colors (alwān) to accidents (akwān) to argue that substances cannot be devoid of them. This is a typical kalām argument using analogy.
This is an analogical argument, not a deductive syllogism.
3
Recognize the reduction to qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid
The author says this method is ultimately 'nothing but reasoning from the unseen to the witnessed' (qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid). Understand that this is a form of analogical reasoning in kalām.
This reduction shows the author's view that the method is not genuinely demonstrative.
4
Understand the rejection in intellectual matters
The passage states that later thinkers (al-nuẓẓār al-mutaʾakhkhirūn) reject this method for maʿqūlāt (intellectual/rational matters) because what is sought there is ʿilm (certain knowledge), and mere agreement or disagreement has no bearing on truth.
This is a key epistemological point: consensus is not a source of certainty in demonstrative sciences.
5
Contrast with legal reasoning
Reflect that this method is accepted in sharʿī (legal) reasoning, where consensus (ijmāʿ) of scholars is a valid source. But in pure intellectual questions, it is not valid.
The author does not say it is invalid everywhere; only in intellectual matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث : الإستدلال بالمتفق عليه على المختلف فيه(١) ويمثلون له بقياس الألوان على الأكوان في استحالة تعري الجواهر عنها . فهذا الطريق - في آخر تحليل له - ليس إلا قياس الغائب على الشاهد . ويعرفه إمام الحرمين في تقاسيمه للنظر الشرعي بالحاق المختلف فيه بالمجتمع المتفق عليه(٢) . أما الاستدلال بالمتفق عليه على المختلف فيه في المعقولات ، فلا يقبله أيضاً النظار المتأخرون . فان المطلوب في المعقولات العلم ، ولا أثر للخلاف والوفاق في المعقولات(٣) .
The third way: Reasoning by what is agreed upon about what is disputed. And they exemplify it by the syllogism of colors on the accidents in the impossibility of substances being devoid of them. So this way – in its final analysis – is nothing but reasoning from the unseen to the witnessed. And Imām al-Ḥaramayn defines it in his divisions of sharʿī reasoning as attaching the disputed to the agreed-upon. As for reasoning by what is agreed upon about what is disputed in intellectual matters, the later thinkers do not accept it. For what is sought in intellectual matters is knowledge, and difference and agreement have no effect in intellectual matters.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage discusses the third method of reasoning: istidlāl bil-muttafaq ʿalayhi ʿalā al-mukhtalaf fīhi, i.e., using a matter on which there is consensus to argue about a disputed matter. The author gives an example: analogizing colors (aʿrāḍ, accidents) to akwān (states of being) in that substances cannot be devoid of them – a typical kalām argument. The author then reduces this method to qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid (analogy from the unseen to the witnessed). Imām al-Ḥaramayn defines it in his work on sharʿī reasoning as attaching the disputed to the agreed-upon. However, the author notes that later thinkers (al-nuẓẓār al-mutaʾakhkhirūn) reject this method in intellectual matters (maʿqūlāt) because what is sought there is certain knowledge (ʿilm), and mere agreement or disagreement is irrelevant to truth in pure intellectual questions. Thus, the method is only acceptable in legal reasoning but not in demonstrative sciences.

Key Terms
الإستدلال بالمتفق عليه على المختلف فيه
