Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Two-Premise Limit in Syllogism
Critique of the two-premise limit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the claim that a syllogism must have exactly two premises, and identify how this critique expands the scope of valid deductive arguments in Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
A syllogism is not limited to two premises; the word 'two' itself allows more.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges a common assertion among logicians that every syllogism is composed of exactly two premises. He examines the Arabic word for 'two' (الإثنين) and shows that it can encompass a greater number. This critique has implications for how we understand complex arguments in fiqh and kalam, where multiple premises are often needed. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Look for the logicians' claim that a syllogism has exactly two premises. The passage says 'but sometimes they include... premises additional to its two premises', indicating the target.
2
Examine the linguistic argument
Ibn Taymiyya analyzes the word 'two' (الإثنين) by considering two interpretations: genus of number (including two and above) and real plural (three or more). Both interpretations allow more than two premises.
3
Identify the consequence
This linguistic analysis implies that the two-premise rule is not absolute. Logicians themselves use compound syllogisms with extra premises, as acknowledged in the passage.
4
Assess the strength of the critique
Ibn Taymiyya's argument is a valid linguistic objection, showing that the term 'two' is ambiguous and cannot strictly limit syllogisms to two premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقط ، لأن لفظ الإثنين إن أريد به جنس العدد كان متناولاً للإثنين فصاعداً ، فيجوز أن يكون القياس مكوناً من ثلاث مقدمات أو أربع أو أكثر ، وإن أرادوا به الجمع الحقيقي تكون من ثلاث مقدمات فصاعداً وفي كلتا الحالتين لا يقتصر القياس على مقدمتين . ولكنهم أحياناً يدرجون في القياس مقدمات زائدة على مقدمتيه ويسمون هذا بالقياس المركب ويرجعونه إلى « أقيسة متعددة سيقت لبيان مطلوب واحد . إلا أن القياس المبين للمطلوب بالذات منها ليس إلا واحداً » . وبقية الأقيسة لبيان مقدمات القياس . وهذه الأقيسة التي تتكون لبيان المقدمات تسمى مفصولة ، إذا صرح فيها بنتائجها ، وموصلة إذا لم يصرح وأحياناً تحذف بعض مقدمات القياس . إما اعتماداً على ذكاء المخاطب وإما للمغالطة . ولعل ابن تيمية يقصد بذلك ما يعبر عنه المناطقة بالقياس المضمر .
Only, because if by the word 'two' is meant the genus of number, it includes two and above, so it is permissible that the syllogism is composed of three premises or four or more. And if they meant by it the real plural, then it is from three premises and above. In both cases, the syllogism is not limited to two premises. But sometimes they include in the syllogism premises additional to its two premises and call this a 'compound syllogism' and reduce it to 'multiple syllogisms brought together to explain a single sought conclusion. Except that the syllogism that explains the sought conclusion by itself is only one of them.' The remaining syllogisms are for explaining the premises of the syllogism. These syllogisms that are formed to explain the premises are called 'disjunct' if their results are stated explicitly, and 'conjunct' if they are not stated. And sometimes some premises of the syllogism are omitted, either relying on the intelligence of the addressee or for sophistry. Perhaps Ibn Taymiyya intends by that what the logicians call 'implicit syllogism'.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of a syllogism (qiyas) and critiques the common logician's claim that it requires exactly two premises. The author first examines the word 'two' (الإثنين): if it means the genus of number, it encompasses two and everything above it, so a syllogism could have three, four, or more premises. If it means the real plural, then it requires at least three premises. In both interpretations, a syllogism is not confined to two premises. The logicians, however, sometimes add extra premises beyond the two basic ones and call this a 'compound syllogism' (القياس المركب), reducing it to multiple syllogisms all serving a single conclusion. The real syllogism that directly yields the sought conclusion is only one; the others clarify its premises. These auxiliary syllogisms are termed 'disjunct' (مفصولة) if they explicitly state their results, and 'conjunct' (موصلة) if they do not. Premises may also be omitted for the sake of brevity or to deceive. Ibn Taymiyya seems to refer here to what logicians call an 'implicit syllogism' (القياس المضمر). For example, in fiqh, one might say 'This liquid is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden, and what is forbidden is sinful, therefore this liquid is sinful'—a compound syllogism with three premises, not two.

مفصولة
موصلة
القياس المضمر

Types of Compound Syllogisms: Disjunct, Conjunct, and Implicit
Types of compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between disjunct (مفصولة) and conjunct (موصلة) compound syllogisms and identify when a syllogism is implicit (مضمر) in Islamic argumentation.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism can be explicit or implicit, and its auxiliary arguments may reveal or hide their conclusions.

Building on the critique of the two-premise limit, Ibn Taymiyya explains how logicians organize compound syllogisms. When multiple syllogisms serve a single conclusion, the supporting ones can be 'disjunct' (explicit in their results) or 'conjunct' (implicit). He also notes that premises may be omitted entirely, creating what logicians call an implicit syllogism. These distinctions are crucial for analyzing the structure of extended arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main syllogism and auxiliary ones
In a compound syllogism, only one syllogism directly yields the sought conclusion; the others serve to explain its premises. The passage states 'the syllogism that explains the sought conclusion by itself is only one of them.'
2
Distinguish between disjunct and conjunct
Disjunct (مفصولة) syllogisms explicitly state their conclusions; conjunct (موصلة) syllogisms do not. This is based on whether the results are stated ('صرح فيها بنتائجها') or not.
3
Understand implicit syllogism
Premises can be omitted either to rely on the audience's intelligence or for sophistry. This omitted-premise form is called implicit syllogism (القياس المضمر).
4
Apply the distinction
Given an example, classify whether it is disjunct, conjunct, or implicit. For instance, 'The world is contingent; everything contingent has a cause; therefore the world has a cause' is fully stated (disjunct). If the second premise is omitted, it becomes implicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ibn Taymiyya on the Variability of Premises and the Middle Term
Author reports and resolves the dispute between Ibn Sina and al-Razi on the sufficiency of two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' assumption that exactly two premises are necessary for a syllogistic conclusion, and identify his concept of the variable middle term.

Core Idea
Proof is flexible: the number of premises depends on the individual's knowledge, not on a fixed syllogistic mold.

Ibn Taymiyya presents the disagreement between Ibn Sina and al-Razi over whether awareness of subsumption is needed beyond the two premises. He diagnoses the root cause: the false assumption that production requires exactly two premises. He then offers his own solution based on the implication (luzum) and the variable middle term.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ibn Sina's example and claim
Ibn Sina gives the example of a person who knows 'this animal is a mule' and 'every mule does not give birth,' yet may doubt when seeing a mule with a swollen belly. He argues that without active awareness of subsumption (that this mule falls under 'every mule'), the conclusion is not drawn.
The example illustrates that knowledge of premises alone may not produce the conclusion if the subsumption is not explicitly recognized.
2
Follow al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi raises a dilemma: if awareness of subsumption is a separate piece of knowledge, it becomes a third premise, leading to circularity or infinite regress. If it is not separate, it cannot be a condition for production, because a condition must be distinct from what it conditions.
Al-Razi's objection challenges the necessity of a third condition while preserving the two-premise framework.
3
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's diagnosis
Ibn Taymiyya identifies the root cause of the dispute: the logicians' assumption that production can only occur from exactly two premises. He argues that this assumption is false and that the number of premises can vary.
Ibn Taymiyya shifts the focus from the number of premises to the nature of the logical implication (luzum).
4
Grasp Ibn Taymiyya's alternative
Ibn Taymiyya proposes that proof depends on recognizing the implication between premises and conclusion, called luzum. The middle term (wasat) is the knowledge that mediates this implication, and it varies among individuals. What suffices as proof for one person may not suffice for another; thus the number of premises can be one, two, or more.
This contrasts with the Aristotelian notion of a fixed middle term.
5
Apply to Islamic sciences
Consider the example: 'Every intoxicant is haram; khamr is an intoxicant; therefore khamr is haram.' A scholar may immediately grasp the implication, while a layman may need an additional premise establishing that khamr is indeed intoxicating. Ibn Taymiyya's principle justifies tailoring proofs to the audience.
The same implication can be reached through different middle terms depending on the individual's prior knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشير ابن تيمية بعد ذلك إلى اختلاف ابن سينا والرازي في أن العلم بالمقدمتين فقط هل يكفي في الإنتاج . أما ابن سينا فيرى أن الإنسان قد يعلم المقدمتين : هذا الحيوان بغلة ، وكل بغلة لا تلد ، ومع ذلك قد يشك حين يرى بغلة منتفخة البطن : هل هي عاقر أم لا ؟ ويرى أنه لا بد مع العلم بالمقدمتين من التفطن لكيفية اندراج المقدمة الجزئية تحت المقدمة الكلية وإلا تعذر الإنتاج . ولا يوافق الرازي على هذا ، لأن اندراج إحدى المقدمتين تحت الأخرى ، إما أن يكون معلوماً مغايراً لتلك المقدمتين ، وإما أن لا يكون فان كان الأول ، أدى هذا إلى وجود مقدمة أخرى ضرورية في الإنتاج . ويكون البحث في كيفية التئامها مع المقدمتين الأخريين كالبحث في كيفية التئام الأوليين بعضهما مع بعض . ويؤدي هذا إلى الدور ـ وإن لم يكن ذلك الاندراج معلوماً مغايراً للمقدمتين ، امتنع أن يكون شرطاً في الإنتاج ، لأن الشرط ينبغي أن يكون مغايراً للمشروط . ولا توجد هنا ثمة مغايرة فانتفت أن تكون شرطاً . أما الخطأ في المقدمتين السالف ذكرهما ، فذلك إنما يمكن إذا كان الحاضر في الذهن إحدى المقدمتين فقط : إما الصغرى وإما الكبرى أما إذا اجتمعت المقدمتان في الذهن ، فلا يمكن الشك في النتيجة أصلاً . لا يوافق ابن تيمية على هذا . بل يعالج المسألة علاجاً آخر فيقرر أن سبب النزاع كله هو ظن المناطقة بأن الإنتاج لا يمكن أن يحدث إلا عن مقدمتين . ولكن يجب التوصل إلى المطلوب بأي عدد ممكن من المقدمات « والمغفول عنه ليس بمعلوم حال الغفلة ، فإذا تذكر صار معلوماً بالفعل » وهذا ما حدث تماماً في المثال السالف الذكر « فوجه الدليل إذن هو العلم بلزوم المدلول له سواء سمي استحضاراً أو تفطناً ، فمتى استحضر في ذهنه لزوم المدلول ، علم أنه دال عليه » . وقد يحدث هذا اللزوم بالضرورة ، وقد يكتفي فيه بمقدمة واحدة وقد يحتاج إلى مقدمتين أو ثلاث أو أكثر ، لأن الوسط وهو العلم بين اللازم والملزوم ، أو بين المحكوم به والمحكوم عليه ، يتنوع ويتعدد تبعاً لتنوع الناس وتعددهم . فليس ما كان وسطاً مستلزماً للحكم في حق إنسان هو الدليل الذي يجب أن يكون وسطاً في حق الآخر ، بل قد يصل إلى الحكم بوسط آخر يحدث هذا في المواد العقلية والحسية ، كما يحدث في الأمور العقلية والمتواترة . وعلى العموم إن قصر الاستدلال بالبرهان على مقدمتين عند ابن تيمية باطل ومخالف للفطرة بل هو نوع من أنواع « التطويل الكثير » . يقول في موافقة صريح المعقول « فبتقدير بيانهم للمقدمتين يكونون قد طولوا او داروا بالعقول دورة تبعد عن العقول معرفة الله تعالى والإقرار بنبواته ولم يلجأ إليه
And Ibn Taymiyya then refers to the disagreement of Ibn Sina and al-Razi concerning whether knowledge of the two premises alone is sufficient for production. Ibn Sina holds that a man may know the two premises: 'This animal is a mule, and every mule does not give birth,' yet he may doubt when he sees a mule with a swollen belly: is it barren or not? He holds that along with knowledge of the two premises, one must be aware of how the particular premise is subsumed under the universal premise; otherwise production is impossible. Al-Razi disagrees with this, because the subsumption of one premise under the other is either a knowledge distinct from those two premises or not. If it is the former, this leads to the existence of another premise necessary for production, and investigating how it coheres with the other two premises is like investigating how the first two cohere with each other, leading to circularity. If that subsumption is not a knowledge distinct from the two premises, then it is impossible for it to be a condition for production, because the condition must be distinct from the conditioned. No distinctness exists here, so it cannot be a condition. As for error in the two aforementioned premises, that can only occur if only one premise is present in the mind: either the minor or the major. But if both premises are together in the mind, doubt in the result is impossible at all. Ibn Taymiyya does not agree with this. Rather, he treats the issue differently and states that the entire dispute is caused by the logicians' assumption that production can only occur from two premises. But one must arrive at the sought conclusion by any possible number of premises. 'What is neglected is not known in the state of negligence; when recalled, it becomes known actually.' This is exactly what happens in the aforementioned example. 'Thus the direction of the proof is knowledge of the implication of the signification, whether called presence of mind or awareness. So whenever one brings to mind the implication of the signification, he knows that it indicates it.' This implication may occur necessarily, and may suffice with one premise, or may need two, three, or more, because the middle term—which is the knowledge between the necessary and the necessitating, or between the judged and the subject of judgment—varies and multiplies according to the variation and multiplicity of people. So what is a middle term necessitating the judgment for one person is not the proof that must be a middle term for another; rather, he may reach the judgment by another middle term. This occurs in intellectual and sensory matters, just as it occurs in rational and widely transmitted matters. In general, restricting demonstration to two premises according to Ibn Taymiyya is false and contrary to human nature; rather, it is a kind of 'great prolongation.' He says in Muwaftiqat al-Ma'qul: 'Given their statement of the two premises, they have prolonged or turned the minds in a cycle that distances the minds from knowledge of God Almighty and acknowledgment of His prophethoods, and they have not resorted to it.'
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critical analysis of the classical dispute between Ibn Sina and al-Razi over whether knowledge of just two premises suffices for a syllogistic conclusion. The author first states Ibn Sina's position: a person may know that 'this animal is a mule' and 'every mule does not give birth,' yet still doubt when seeing a swollen-belly mule whether it is barren. Ibn Sina argues that one must be attentive to the subsumption (indiraj) of the particular under the universal—i.e., realizing that the individual mule is included in the universal mule category. Without this awareness, the conclusion 'this mule does not give birth' is not drawn. Al-Razi counters: if that subsumption awareness is a separate piece of knowledge, then it becomes a third premise, leading to an infinite regress or circularity. If it is not distinct, then it cannot be a condition for production, since a condition must differ from what it conditions. Error, al-Razi says, occurs only when one premise is forgotten; with both present in mind, the conclusion is inevitable. Ibn Taymiyya rejects both. He identifies the root fallacy: the logicians' assumption that production requires exactly two premises. Instead, the number of premises depends on the person. In the mule example, the 'neglected' knowledge of subsumption, when recalled, becomes actual knowledge; the proof is simply recognizing the implication (luzum) between the premises and conclusion. This implication may be immediate (one premise suffices) or require multiple premises, because the middle term (wasat) varies among individuals. For example, one person might need an extra premise to link 'mule' and 'barrenness,' while another may grasp it directly. This principle holds in intellectual and sensory matters, including theological proofs. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that restricting demonstration (burhan) to two premises contradicts human nature and is an unnecessary prolongation that hinders true knowledge of God and prophethood.

Key Terms
اندراج
وسط
لزوم
برهان

Key Takeaway

Ibn Taymiyya on the Number of Premises in Demonstration
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the logicians' two-premise requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the two-premise requirement and evaluate its validity using evidence from his own examples.

Core Idea
The number of premises in demonstration is not fixed; it varies by context and by the nature of the implication.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a foundational tenet of Aristotelian logic: that every valid inference (intāj) requires exactly two premises. In this lesson, we will trace his argument that this restriction is both unnecessary and contrary to human nature (fiṭra). You will see how he uses examples from Islamic thinkers like al-Rāzī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn to support a more flexible approach to reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Ibn Taymiyya attributes to the logicians the claim that inference (intāj) can only occur from two premises (muqaddimatān). Locate where he states this is the cause of the dispute.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counterclaim
He asserts that the goal is to reach the conclusion with any number of premises, and that the middle term (al-wasaṭ) linking the sign to the signified varies among individuals.
Notice he uses the concept of 'luzūm' (implication) as the basis.
3
Analyze the evidence he provides
He cites al-Rāzī's five proofs for the Creator (each from one premise), Imām al-Ḥaramayn's validation of single-premise inference, and Ibn al-Nawbakhtī's example of 'human as substance' without a second premise.
These are Islamic science examples from kalam and logic.
4
Evaluate the internal consistency
Check if Ibn Taymiyya's own argument uses a fixed number of premises. He does not; he uses multiple examples to show variety, avoiding self-contradiction.
His critique is internally valid.

لا يوافق ابن تيمية على هذا . بل يعالج المسألة علاجاً آخر فيقرر أن سبب النزاع كله هو ظن المناطقة بأن الإنتاج لا يمكن أن يحدث إلا عن مقدمتين . ولكن يجب التوصل إلى المطلوب بأي عدد ممكن من المقدمات « والمغفول عنه ليس بمعلوم حال الغفلة ، فإذا تذكر صار معلوماً بالفعل » وهذا ما حدث تماماً في المثال السالف الذكر « فوجه الدليل إذن هو العلم بلزوم المدلول له سواء سمي استحضاراً أو تفطناً ، فمتى استحضر في ذهنه لزوم المدلول ، علم أنه دال عليه » . وقد يحدث هذا اللزوم بالضرورة ، وقد يكتفي فيه بمقدمة واحدة وقد يحتاج إلى مقدمتين أو ثلاث أو أكثر ، لأن الوسط وهو العلم بين اللازم والملزوم ، أو بين المحكوم به والمحكوم عليه ، يتنوع ويتعدد تبعاً لتنوع الناس وتعددهم . فليس ما كان وسطاً مستلزماً للحكم في حق إنسان هو الدليل الذي يجب أن يكون وسطاً في حق الآخر ، بل قد يصل إلى الحكم بوسط آخر يحدث هذا في المواد العقلية والحسية ، كما يحدث في الأمور العقلية والمتواترة . وعلى العموم إن قصر الاستدلال بالبرهان على مقدمتين عند ابن تيمية باطل ومخالف للفطرة بل هو نوع من أنواع « التطويل الكثير » . يقول في موافقة صريح المعقول « فبتقدير بيانهم للمقدمتين يكونون قد طولوا او داروا بالعقول دورة تبعد عن العقول معرفة الله تعالى والإقرار بنبواته ولم يلجأ إليه نظار المسلمين بل كانت طريقتهم « أن يذكروا من الأدلة على المقدمات ما يحتاجون إليه ولا يلتزمون في كل استدلال أن يذكروا مقدمتين كما يفعله من يسلك سبيل المنطقيين » . ويذكر ابن تيمية أن الرازي أثبت الصانع بخمسة مسالك ، كلها مبنية على مقدمة واحدة ، كما يذكر صاحب البحر المحيط أنه يؤثر عن إمام الحرمين أنه يصح إنتاج المقدمة الواحدة ويقول صاحب نقد النثر « وليس يجب القياس إلا عن قول يتقدم ، فيكون القياس نتيجة ذلك ، كقولنا إذا كان الحي حساساً متحركاً ، فالإنسان حي . وربما كان ذلك في اللسان العربي مقدمة أو مقدمتين أو أكثر على قدر ما يتجه من إفهام المخاطب » ويذكر في فقرة أخرى « وإنما يكتفي في لسان العرب بمقدمة واحدة على التوسع وعلم المخاطب » . وفي فقرة من كتاب الآراء والديانات لابن النوبختي وهي التي ينقلها ابن تيمية إلينا في كتابه ، اعتراض بعض نظار المسلمين على القول بأن القياس لا ينتج من مقدمة واحدة . ويقرر أنه ـ عندهم يمكن الإستدلال على أن الإنسان جوهر ، من غير أن يوضع الإستدلال في صورة قياس فالدليل على أن الإنسان جوهر ، أنه يقبل المتضادات في أزمان مختلفة . وليست هناك أدنى حاجة إلى ذكر مقدمة ثانية وهي أن كل قابل للمتضادات في أزمان مختلفة فهو جوهر ، لأن الخاص داخل في العام . ودلالة إحدى المقدمتين على المطلوب تغني عن دلالة المقدمة الأخرى . ويتحقق هذا في جميع صور الإستدلال البرهاني ولا توجد قضيتان كليتان يستدل بهما على صحة نتيجة ، لأن القائل إذا قال الجوهر لكل حي ، والحياة لكل إنسان ـ فالنتيجة أن الجوهر لكل إنسان هنا تكرار لا معنى له ، أو مصادرة على المطلوب ، لأنه سواء في العقل قول
Ibn Taymiyya does not agree with this. Rather, he treats the issue in another way and establishes that the entire cause of the dispute is the logicians' assumption that inference (intāj) can only occur from two premises. But one must arrive at the desired conclusion by any possible number of premises. 'And what is neglected is not known during the state of neglect, so when it is remembered it becomes known in actuality.' This is exactly what happened in the aforementioned example. 'Thus the basis of the proof is the knowledge of the implication of the thing signified to the sign, whether it be called bringing to mind or paying attention. So when one brings to mind the implication of the thing signified, he knows that it indicates it.' This implication may occur necessarily, and one premise may suffice for it, or it may need two, three, or more premises, because the middle term—namely, the knowledge between the necessary consequence and the necessitating thing, or between the subject of judgment and the object of judgment—varies and multiplies according to the variation and multiplicity of people. So what is a middle term entailing the judgment for one person is not necessarily the proof that must be a middle term for another; rather, one may reach the judgment through another middle term. This occurs in mental and sensory matters, just as it occurs in intellectual and recurrent matters. In general, restricting demonstration (burhān) to two premises is, according to Ibn Taymiyya, false and contrary to natural disposition (fiṭra); indeed it is a type of 'great prolixity.' He says in Muwāfaqat Ṣarīḥ al-Maʿqūl: 'Supposing they present the two premises, they would have been prolix and turned minds in a loop that distances the minds from knowledge of Allah Almighty and affirmation of His prophecies. The Muslim thinkers did not resort to that; rather, their way was to mention from the proofs of the premises what they needed, and they did not commit themselves in every inference to mentioning two premises, as does he who follows the path of the logicians.' And Ibn Taymiyya mentions that al-Rāzī proved the Creator by five ways, all based on a single premise. Likewise, the author of al-Baḥr al-Muḥīṭ mentions that it is related from Imām al-Ḥaramayn that inference from a single premise is valid. And the author of Naqd al-Nathr says: 'The syllogism (qiyās) is only necessary from a preceding statement, so the syllogism is the result of that, as when we say: if the living being is sensitive and moving, then the human is a living being. And perhaps this, in the Arabic tongue, may be one premise or two or more according to the understanding of the addressee.' And he mentions in another paragraph: 'In the tongue of the Arabs they suffice with a single premise due to extensiveness and the addressee's knowledge.' And in a paragraph from the book al-Ārāʾ wa al-Diyānāt by Ibn al-Nawbakhtī, which Ibn Taymiyya transmits to us in his book, there is an objection by some Muslim thinkers to the statement that the syllogism cannot yield a conclusion from a single premise. He affirms that, in their view, one can deduce that the human is a substance without placing the deduction in the form of a syllogism. So the proof that the human is a substance is that it accepts opposites at different times. And there is not the least need to mention a second premise, namely that everything that accepts opposites at different times is a substance, because the specific is included in the general. The indication of one of the two premises for the required conclusion suffices over the indication of the other premise. This is realized in all forms of demonstrative inference (istidlāl burhānī). And there are no two universal propositions that are used to infer the validity of a conclusion, because if someone says: 'Substance belongs to every living being, and life belongs to every human'—then the conclusion that substance belongs to every human is a meaningless repetition or a begging of the question (muṣādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb), because the statement is equivalent in the mind.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the logicians' insistence that valid reasoning (intāj) requires exactly two premises. He begins by identifying the root of the dispute: the logicians' assumption that inference cannot occur except from two premises. Ibn Taymiyya counters that the goal is to reach the required conclusion, and any number of premises—one, two, three, or more—can serve, depending on the context. He supports this with a quotation about the nature of knowledge: what is neglected is unknown during neglect, but when remembered, it becomes actual knowledge. In the earlier example (the reader is referred to a prior discussion), the proof is simply the awareness of the implication (luzūm) between the sign and the signified, whether one calls it 'bringing to mind' or 'paying attention.' This implication can be necessary, and sometimes one premise suffices; other times more are needed. The reason for variation is the 'middle term' (al-wasaṭ), which is the knowledge linking the necessary consequence (lāzim) to the necessitating thing (malzūm), or between the subject and predicate of the judgment. People differ in what serves as a middle term for them; what is a sufficient proof for one person may not be for another. This applies to both rational and sensory matters. Ibn Taymiyya then declares that restricting demonstrative reasoning (burhān) to exactly two premises is false, contrary to natural human disposition (fiṭra), and amounts to 'great prolixity' (al-taṭwīl al-kathīr). He quotes his own work Muwāfaqat Ṣarīḥ al-Maʿqūl, arguing that if logicians present their obligatory two premises, they are being unnecessarily long and turning minds in a loop that distances them from knowledge of God and His prophets. Muslim thinkers (nuẓẓār al-muslimīn) did not adopt this method; rather, they mentioned only the premises needed without committing to any fixed number. As support, he notes that al-Rāzī proved the existence of the Creator using five distinct ways (masālik), each built on a single premise. He also cites al-Zarkashī (author of al-Baḥr al-Muḥīṭ) who relates from Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) that inference from a single premise is valid. Further, the author of Naqd al-Nathr (Ibn Qudāma) states that a syllogism (qiyās) does not necessarily require a preceding statement? Rather, the syllogism is the result of that statement, e.g., 'If the living being is sensitive and moving, then the human is a living being.' In Arabic, this can be expressed in one, two, or more premises depending on the addressee's understanding. The Arabic tongue often suffices with a single premise due to linguistic extensiveness and the audience's prior knowledge. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya transmits a passage from Ibn al-Nawbakhtī's book al-Ārāʾ wa al-Diyānāt, where some Muslim thinkers object to the idea that a syllogism cannot produce a conclusion from one premise. They argue that one can prove that the human is a substance without casting the argument in syllogistic form: the proof is simply that the human accepts opposites at different times. There is no need to add the universal premise 'Everything that accepts opposites at different times is a substance' because the specific case is already included in the general. The indication of one premise suffices. In all demonstrative inferences, there are no two universal propositions used to infer a conclusion; if someone says 'Substance belongs to every living being, and life belongs to every human,' the conclusion 'Substance belongs to every human' is either a meaningless repetition or a circular argument (muṣādara). Thus, the logicians' two-premise requirement is flawed.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Two-Premise Requirement
Author presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the two-premise requirement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the mandatory two-premise syllogistic structure and evaluate its implications for Aristotelian logic.

Core Idea
The necessity of two premises is a Greek imposition, not a natural reasoning requirement.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the fundamental Aristotelian rule that every syllogism must have exactly two premises. Drawing on earlier Muslim theologians, he argues that inference often works with a single premise, especially in Arabic discourse. This lesson unpacks his critique and its radical implications for the entire syllogistic edifice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
The Aristotelian requirement that a categorical syllogism must consist of exactly two premises. Note that Ibn Taymiyya is not rejecting syllogism itself, but the mandatory two-premise structure.
This is the claim under attack.
2
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's evidence
He cites al-Razi's five proofs for the Creator, each built on one premise, and reports that Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) considered a single premise sufficient. He also quotes Arabic linguistic practice where a premise may be omitted due to context.
Note the diversity of sources: theology, logic, and language.
3
Analyze the example from Ibn al-Nawbakhti
The proof that man is a substance: 'man accepts opposites at different times' – this alone suffices because 'everything that accepts opposites at different times is a substance' is implicit. The specific is contained in the general.
This illustrates the principle of implicit premises.
4
Understand the collapse argument
If the first figure does not require two premises, all other figures reduce to it, so the entire Aristotelian syllogistic collapses. The author states this explicitly.
This shows the high stakes of the critique.
5
Assess the epistemological shift
Ibn Taymiyya advocates for freeing the intellect from artificial laws, allowing reasoning to flow naturally based on context and audience understanding.
This is a broader methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نظار المسلمين بل كانت طريقتهم « أن يذكروا من الأدلة على المقدمات ما يحتاجون إليه ولا يلتزمون في كل استدلال أن يذكروا مقدمتين كما يفعله من يسلك سبيل المنطقيين »(١) . ويذكر ابن تيمية أن الرازي أثبت الصانع بخمسة(٢) مسالك ، كلها مبنية على مقدمة واحدة ، كما يذكر صاحب البحر المحيط أنه يؤثر عن إمام الحرمين أنه يصح إنتاج المقدمة الواحدة(٣) ويقول صاحب نقد النثر « وليس يجب القياس إلا عن قول يتقدم ، فيكون القياس نتيجة ذلك ، كقولنا إذا كان الحي حساساً متحركاً ، فالإنسان حي . وربما كان ذلك في اللسان العربي مقدمة أو مقدمتين أو أكثر على قدر ما يتجه من إفهام المخاطب » ويذكر في فقرة أخرى « وإنما يكتفي في لسان العرب بمقدمة واحدة على التوسع وعلم المخاطب »(٤) . وفي فقرة من كتاب الآراء والديانات لابن النوبختي وهي التي ينقلها ابن تيمية إلينا في كتابه ، اعتراض بعض نظار المسلمين على القول بأن القياس لا ينتج من مقدمة واحدة . ويقرر أنه ـ عندهم يمكن الإستدلال على أن الإنسان جوهر ، من غير أن يوضع الإستدلال في صورة قياس فالدليل على أن الإنسان جوهر ، أنه يقبل المتضادات في أزمان مختلفة . وليست هناك أدنى حاجة إلى ذكر مقدمة ثانية وهي أن كل قابل للمتضادات في أزمان مختلفة فهو جوهر ، لأن الخاص داخل في العام . ودلالة إحدى المقدمتين على المطلوب تغني عن دلالة المقدمة الأخرى . ويتحقق هذا في جميع صور الإستدلال البرهاني ولا توجد قضيتان كليتان يستدل بهما على صحة نتيجة ، لأن القائل إذا قال الجوهر لكل حي ، والحياة لكل إنسان ـ فالنتيجة أن الجوهر لكل إنسان هنا تكرار لا معنى له ، أو مصادرة على المطلوب ، لأنه سواء في العقل قول القائل : الجوهر لكل حي والجوهر لكل إنسان ، لأن إحدى تلك المقدمتين تغني عن الأخرى تماماً . وليس هناك « من المطالب العلمية ما يتوقف المطلوب فيه على مقدمتين بينتين بأنفسهما ، وإذا ما كان الأمر كذلك ، كانت إحداهما كافية كما أنه ليست هناك مقدمتان أوليتان لا تحتاجان إلى برهان يتقدمهما يستدل بهما على شيء مختلف فيه ، وتكون المقدمتان في العقول أولى بالقبول من النتيجة ، بل لا بد من مقدمات عدة تشرح ما اختلف فيه(١) . وإذا ما أثبتنا خطأ توقف الشكل الأول على مقدمتين ـ انهدم القياس الأرسططاليسي من أساسه ـ فالشكل الأول أكمل تلك الأشكال ، وبقية الأشكال المختلفة يمكن ردها بسهولة إليه . وأخيراً نستطيع أن نقرر أن اعتراض ابن تيمية على اقتصار القياس على مقدمتين هو اتجاه عربي صرف يحاول به نقد المنطق الأرسططاليسي ، من حيث أنه يسلك بالفكر في طرق متعددة متكلفة ، بينما ينبغي لديه إطلاق الفكر على سجيته وعدم تقييده بقوانين صناعية .
The Muslim theologians' method was that they mention from the evidence for the premises what they need, and they do not commit themselves in every inference to mentioning two premises as does one who follows the path of the logicians. Ibn Taymiyya mentions that al-Razi proved the Creator by five paths, all built on one premise. Likewise, the author of al-Bahr al-Muhit reports that it is transmitted from Imam al-Haramayn that the production (of a conclusion) from a single premise is valid. The author of Naqd al-Nathr says: 'A syllogism is only necessitated by a preceding statement, so the syllogism is a result of that, like our saying: if the living is sensitive and moving, then the human is living. And perhaps that in the Arabic tongue is one premise or two premises or more according to what suits the understanding of the addressee.' And he mentions in another paragraph: 'In the Arabic tongue, one suffices with a single premise due to expansion and the knowledge of the addressee.' And in a paragraph from the book al-Ara' wa al-Diyanat by Ibn al-Nawbakhti, which Ibn Taymiyya transmits to us in his book, (there is) an objection of some Muslim theologians to the statement that the syllogism does not produce from a single premise. And he affirms that — according to them — one can infer that man is a substance without placing the inference in the form of a syllogism. The proof that man is a substance is that he accepts opposites at different times. And there is no need at all to mention a second premise, namely that everything that accepts opposites at different times is a substance, because the specific is included in the general. The indication of one of the two premises for the required conclusion suffices over the indication of the other premise. And this is realized in all forms of demonstrative inference. There are no two universal propositions by which one infers the correctness of a result, because if the speaker says: 'Substance belongs to every living thing, and life belongs to every human' — the result that substance belongs to every human is here a meaningless repetition or a begging of the question, because in the intellect the statement of the speaker: 'Substance belongs to every living thing and substance belongs to every human' are the same, since one of those two premises completely suffices over the other. And there is no 'among the scientific demands what requires the required to depend on two self-evident premises, and if the matter is like that, then one of them is sufficient. Also, there are no two primary premises that do not need a proof preceding them, by which one infers something disputed, and the two premises in the intellects are more acceptable than the result. Rather, there must be several premises that explain what is disputed.' And if we establish the error of the dependence of the first figure on two premises, the Aristotelian syllogism collapses from its foundation — for the first figure is the most perfect of those figures, and the rest of the different figures can be easily reduced to it. Finally, we can state that Ibn Taymiyya's objection to restricting the syllogism to two premises is a purely Arab direction by which he attempts to critique Aristotelian logic, from the perspective that it leads the intellect into multiple laborious paths, while according to him the intellect should be released naturally and not restricted by artificial laws.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian requirement that a syllogism must consist of exactly two premises. It opens by quoting that Muslim theologians (nuzzar) did not always use two premises; they mentioned only what was necessary. Ibn Taymiyya cites al-Razi's proofs for the Creator, each based on one premise, and reports from al-Zarkashi (author of al-Bahr al-Muhit) that Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) considered a single premise sufficient for a conclusion. Qudama in Naqd al-Nathr supports this, giving the example: 'If the living is sensitive and moving, then the human is living' — this is a syllogism from one premise, because the other premise is understood. The author then relates Ibn al-Nawbakhti's objection to the idea that a syllogism cannot produce from one premise: one can prove that man is a substance by noting that he accepts opposites over time, without needing the second premise explicitly, because the specific is contained in the general. The argument is that two universal premises often lead to redundancy or circularity; for example, 'Substance belongs to every living thing' and 'Substance belongs to every human' are essentially the same claim. The author concludes that if the first figure's need for two premises is disproved, the entire Aristotelian syllogistic collapses, since all other figures reduce to the first. Finally, the author summarizes that Ibn Taymiyya's objection is a purely Arab direction aimed at liberating thought from artificial constraints, preferring natural reasoning.

قياس
شكل أول

D) No change occurs since logic does not rely on figures.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique challenges the Aristotelian orthodoxy by arguing that valid syllogistic reasoning does not always require two explicit premises, a view grounded in Arabic linguistic practice and the methods of early Muslim theologians.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Three Inferences
Author outlines Ibn Taymiyya's examination of syllogism, analogy, and induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Aristotelian types of inference and explain Ibn Taymiyya's three-fold critique of their classification.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya challenges not the validity but the exclusivity and epistemic ranking of the three Aristotelian inferences.

This lesson introduces Ibn Taymiyya's critical examination of the Aristotelian division of inference. Aristotle claimed that only three types exist: categorical syllogism (yielding certainty), analogy (yielding opinion), and induction (complete certain, incomplete opinion). Ibn Taymiyya questions these claims from three angles: their epistemic value, whether Muslims already knew them, and whether the list is exhaustive. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to assess the strength of inferences in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the three Aristotelian inferences
Identify the three types: qiyas al-shumul (categorical syllogism), qiyas al-tamthil (analogy), and al-istiqra' (induction). Note their Arabic names.
2
Extract the epistemic ranking
According to the passage, which types yield certainty (yaqin) and which yield opinion (zann)? Categorical and complete induction yield certainty; analogy and incomplete induction yield opinion.
3
Outline Ibn Taymiyya's three angles of critique
He examines (1) whether each actually delivers its claimed certainty, (2) whether Muslims were already familiar with them before Greek logic, and (3) whether these three exhaust all forms of inference.
This sets the stage for a deeper critique of the uniqueness of the Aristotelian framework.
4
Assess the critique's significance
Consider: If Ibn Taymiyya is correct that the classification is incomplete or that certainty can be achieved through other means, then the monopoly of Greek logic is broken. This opens the door for other methods (e.g., revelation, tawatur, etc.) to be considered valid inferences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - نقد صور الاستدلال الأرسططاليسية : قسم المنطق الأرسططاليسي أنواع الاستدلال إلى ثلاثة : ١ - قياس الشمول ٢ - قياس التمثيل ٣ - الإستقراء . واعتبر القياس الأول موصلاً إلى اليقين ، والثاني إلى الظن ، والثالث ينقسم إلى قسمين إستقراء تام وهو يؤدي إلى اليقين ، واستقراء ناقص وهو يؤدي إلى الظن ، وحاول ابن تيمية أن يبحث هذه الأنواع الثلاثة من ثلاثة نواح : ١ - من حيث إيصال البعض منها إلى اليقين والبعض منها إلى الظن . ٢ - من ناحية معرفة المسلمين لها . ٣ - من حيث حصر صور الاستدلال في ثلاثة فقط .
4 - Critique of Aristotelian forms of inference: Aristotelian logic divided the types of inference into three: 1- Syllogism of inclusion (qiyas al-shumul), 2- Analogical syllogism (qiyas al-tamthil), 3- Induction (al-istiqra'). It considered the first type as leading to certainty, the second to opinion, and the third is divided into two parts: complete induction which leads to certainty, and incomplete induction which leads to opinion. Ibn Taymiyya attempted to examine these three types from three aspects: 1- From the perspective of some leading to certainty and some to opinion. 2- From the aspect of the Muslims' knowledge of them. 3- From the aspect of limiting the forms of inference to only three.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage introduces Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian classification of inference. It states that Aristotle divided inference into three types: (1) qiyas al-shumul (categorical syllogism), (2) qiyas al-tamthil (analogy), and (3) istiqra' (induction). According to Aristotle, the first yields certainty, the second yields only opinion (zann), and induction is subdivided into complete induction (certain) and incomplete induction (opinion). Ibn Taymiyya then examines these three types from three angles: first, their epistemic value (certainty vs. opinion); second, whether Muslims were already familiar with them before Greek logic; and third, whether the classification exhausts all forms of inference. The passage sets up the framework for Ibn Taymiyya's detailed critique, which questions the uniqueness and completeness of the Aristotelian scheme.

Key Terms

قياس التمثيل
الإستقراء

D) Incomplete induction.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique challenges the Aristotelian monopoly on inference by questioning its epistemic claims, historical precedence, and completeness.

?
Consider the fiqh argument: 'Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates; grape juice is not intoxicating, so it is not prohibited.' Is this an inference by categorical syllogism, analogy, or induction? According to Ibn Taymiyya, does its epistemic status change? Explain using the three angles.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Defense of Analogical Reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya critiques Aristotelian dismissal of analogy and sets conditions for its certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions Ibn Taymiyya sets for analogical reasoning to yield certainty and distinguish them from the Aristotelian critique.

Core Idea
For Ibn Taymiyya, analogical reasoning yields certainty when the common feature is proven to be a cause via induction.

Ibn Taymiyya defends analogical reasoning (tamthil) against Aristotelian dismissal. He argues that certainty in tamthil can be stronger than in categorical syllogism, provided the common attribute is established as a cause (illa) through methods like sabr wa taqsim, munasaba, or dawaran.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Aristotelian critique
The logicians gave the example of the heavens being composed like humans. They claimed analogical reasoning is weak and does not yield certainty.
This is the famous example used by Aristotelians.
2
Ibn Taymiyya's counter-critique
The same objections apply if the argument is framed as a categorical syllogism. The weakness lies not in the form but in the evidence for the universal premise.
3
Identify the condition for certainty in tamthil
It is not enough that two particulars share an attribute; one must prove that attribute necessitates the judgment. This common attribute is the 'wasf mushtarak' and acts as the middle term.
Ibn Taymiyya calls this the demand of the effectiveness of the attribute on the judgment.
4
Recognize the methods (masalik al-illa)
Investigation and division (sabr wa taqsim), suitability (munasaba), and concomitance (dawaran) are the methods that lead to certainty in both tamthil and shumul.
These are borrowed from usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يذكر ابن تيمية أن المناطقة الأرسططاليسيين يمثلون للتمثيل بالمثال المشهور : السماء مؤلفة فتكون محدثة قياساً على الإنسان ثم يوردون على هذا القياس ما يثبت ضعفه . مع أنه لو قيل السماء مؤلفة ، وكل مؤلف محدث ، لأمكن توجيه تلك الاعتراضات إليه بعينها . بل إن ابن تيمية يذهب إلى القول بأن اليقين أقوى في قياس تمثيل منه في قياس الشمول ، لأن الحكم يكون ثابتاً في الأصل ، أي علم ثبوته في بعض الجزئيات والجزئيات هي الحقائق الوحيدة الموجودة في الأعيان . ثم يرى أنه لا يكفي في قياس التمثيل إثبات الحكم في أحد الجزئيين لثبوته في الجزئي الآخر ، لاشتراكهما في أمر لم يقم دليل على استلزامه للحكم ؛ بل لا بد من العلم بأن المشترك بين الجزئيين ، مستلزم للحكم والمشترك بينهما هو الحد الأوسط . ويسمى عند الأصوليين - المطالبة بتأثير الوصف في الحكم ، إلا أن الاعتراضات تتوجه نحوها فإما أن تمنع الوصف في الأصل ، وإما أن تمنع الحكم في الأصل وإما أن تمنع الوصف في الفرع ، وإما أن تمنع كون الوصف علة في الحكم . وينكر أن يكون الوصف المذكور علة أو دليل علة . فلا بد أن يلجأ إلى دليل من سبر وتقسيم أو مناسبة أو دوران . وهذه المسالك كافية أن تؤدي إلى اليقين تماماً . وبهذا يمتاز قياس الشاهد على الغائب عن قياس الشمول . وصل ابن تيمية إذن إلى النتيجة التي وصل إليها المحدثون ، من أن قياس التمثيل إذا ما استند على قوانين الإستقراء العلي - وهي التي كان يسميها المسلمون مسالك العلة - أدى إلى نتائج علمية صحيحة . ولكن ابن تيمية يقول إن هذه المسالك التي دلت على أن الوصف المشترك وهو العلة أو دليلها مستلزم للحكم ، تدل أيضاً في قياس الشمول على « أن مستلزم الحد الأوسط للأكبر ، وهو الدال على صحة المقدمة الكبرى : فإن أثبت العلة كان برهان له ، وإن أثبت دليلها كان برهان دلالة ، وإن لم تفد العلم بل أفادت الظن ، فكذلك المقدمة الكبرى في ذلك القياس لا تكون إلا ظنية » . فقياس التمثيل إذن يكاد يكون أقوى من قياس الشمول ، بل إن ابن تيمية يعتبره أيضاً أصلاً لقياس الشمول « القياس التمثيلي هو أصل للقياس الشمولي » والسبب في هذا أن قياس الشمول يستند على قضية كلية وابن تيمية يرى أن طريق التوصل إلى هذه القضية الكلية هو قياس التمثيل ، فيرى أن العلم بالقضية الكلية لا بد له من سبب ، فإن عرفوها باعتبار الغائب بالشاهد ، وأن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ، كما إذا عرفنا أن هذه النار محرقة ، علمنا أن النار الغائبة محرقة لأنها مثلها ، وحكم الشيء حكم مثله ـ وهذا قياس تمثيل . وفي موضع آخر يذكر أن النفس تحكم بأن ذلك الغائب مثل ذلك الشاهد أو أنه يساويه في السبب الموجب له . ويسميه قياس التمثيل والتعليل ويذكر أنه مما يحتج به الفقهاء في إثبات الأحكام الشرعية . ويرى ابن تيمية أن نظار المسلمين استخدموا قياس التمثيل في العقليات ، كما استخدم الفقهاء قياس الشمول في الفقهيات ، لأنه إذا ثبت أن الوصف مستلزم للحكم ، كان هذا دليلاً في جميع العلوم . وحيث لا يستدل بالقياس التمثيلي ، لا يستدل بالقياس الشمولي ولا يعتبر القياس حقيقة في قياس التمثيل ، مجازاً في قياس الشمول ، كما يرى الغزالي والمقدسي - ولا بالعكس كما يرى ابن حزم - بل هو في الإثنين حقيقة ، والقياس العقلي يتناولهما جميعاً ، وحقيقته في كل منهما واحدة ، والخلاف في صورة الإستدلال . ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن ابن تيمية في اعتباره لقياس الغائب على الشاهد موصلاً إلى اليقين ، يردد الفكرة التي أخذ بها نظار المسلمين قبل القرن الخامس . فقد اعتبر جميع النظار - ما عدا مفكرين قلائل كأبي المعالي كما
Ibn Taymiyya mentions that the Aristotelian logicians present for analogical reasoning (tamthil) the famous example: the heavens are composed, so they are temporally originated, by analogy to man. Then they raise against this syllogism what proves its weakness. However, if one had said 'the heavens are composed, and everything composed is temporally originated,' those same objections would also be directed at it. Rather, Ibn Taymiyya goes so far as to say that certainty is stronger in analogical reasoning than in categorical syllogism, because the judgment is established in the original, i.e., knowledge of its existence is in some particulars, and particulars are the only realities existing in concrete individuals. Then he holds that in analogical reasoning it is not sufficient to prove the judgment in one of the two particulars because it is established in the other particular, due to their sharing in a matter for which no evidence exists that it necessitates the judgment; rather, it is necessary to know that the common feature between the two particulars necessitates the judgment, and the common feature between them is the middle term. And among the legal theorists it is called 'demanding the effectiveness of the attribute on the judgment,' except that objections are directed at it: either they prevent the attribute in the original, or they prevent the judgment in the original, or they prevent the attribute in the branch, or they prevent the attribute being the cause of the judgment. And he denies that the mentioned attribute is a cause (illa) or evidence of a cause. So one must resort to evidence from [methods like] investigation and division (sabr wa taqsim), or suitability (munasaba), or concomitance (dawaran). And these paths are sufficient to lead to certainty completely. And by this, the syllogism of the witnessed upon the absent is distinguished from categorical syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya thus arrived at the conclusion reached by the moderns, that analogical reasoning, if it relies on the higher laws of induction – which the Muslims used to call 'paths of the cause' – leads to correct scientific results. But Ibn Taymiyya says: these paths which have indicated that the common attribute, which is the cause or its evidence, necessitates the judgment, also indicate in categorical syllogism that 'the necessitation of the middle term for the major term, which indicates the correctness of the major premise: if it establishes the cause, it is a demonstration (burhan) of it, and if it establishes its evidence, it is a demonstrative proof (burhan dalaala), and if it does not provide knowledge but provides conjecture, then likewise the major premise in that syllogism is nothing but conjectural.' So analogical reasoning is almost stronger than categorical syllogism, rather Ibn Taymiyya also considers it a foundation for categorical syllogism: 'Analogical reasoning is the foundation for categorical syllogism.' The reason is that categorical syllogism is based on a universal proposition, and Ibn Taymiyya holds that the way to arrive at this universal proposition is analogical reasoning. He holds that knowledge of the universal proposition must have a cause; for if they know it by considering the absent in light of the present, and that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like – just as when we know that this fire is burning, we know that the absent fire is burning because it is like it, and the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like – this is analogical reasoning. In another place, he mentions that the soul judges that the absent is like the present or that it equals it in the cause that necessitates it. And he calls it analogical reasoning and causal reasoning and mentions that it is what the jurists use in establishing legal rulings. Ibn Taymiyya holds that the Muslim speculative theologians used analogical reasoning in intellectual matters, just as the jurists used categorical syllogism in legal matters, because if it is established that the attribute necessitates the judgment, this is evidence in all sciences. And where analogical reasoning is not used as evidence, categorical syllogism is not used as evidence. And he does not consider reasoning to be literal in analogical reasoning and metaphorical in categorical syllogism, as al-Ghazali and al-Maqdisi hold – nor the opposite, as Ibn Hazm holds – rather it is literal in both, and rational reasoning encompasses both, and its reality in each is one, and the difference is in the form of inference. It should be noted that Ibn Taymiyya, in his consideration of the syllogism of the absent upon the present as yielding certainty, echoes the idea adopted by Muslim speculative theologians before the fifth century [AH]. For all the speculative theologians considered – except for a few thinkers like Abu al-Ma'ali, as...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critical evaluation of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) versus categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul). The author begins by noting that Aristotelians criticize analogical reasoning using the example of the heavens being composite like humans, but Ibn Taymiyya points out that the same objections apply if the argument were framed as a categorical syllogism. He then makes his central claim: certainty is stronger in analogical reasoning because its judgment is directly known in particular instances, which are the only real existents. For a valid analogy, one must know that the common feature (al-wasf al-mushtarak) – which is the middle term – necessitates the judgment. This parallels the legal theorists' requirement that the attribute be effective in the judgment, with possible objections concerning the attribute in the original or branch, the judgment, or the attribute's causality. Ibn Taymiyya insists that the attribute alone is not enough; one must have evidence from methods like investigation and division (sabr wa taqsim), suitability (munasaba), or concomitance (dawaran) – these are the 'paths of the cause' (masalik al-illa) that lead to certainty. Thus, the syllogism of the witnessed upon the absent (qiyas al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib) is distinguished from categorical syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya then argues that these same paths also apply in categorical syllogism to verify that the middle term necessitates the major, and if they yield only probability, the major premise too is probable. Hence, analogical reasoning is nearly stronger and is actually the foundation of categorical syllogism, because universal premises are derived from induction via analogy. For example, knowing that 'this fire burns' leads, by analogy of similarity, to the universal rule that 'all fire burns.' Ibn Taymiyya notes that both Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) and jurists (fuqaha) use these forms interchangeably, and he rejects claims that one form is literal and the other metaphorical – both are equally valid in their reality, differing only in the form of inference. Finally, the passage observes that Ibn Taymiyya's view on the certainty of analogical reasoning echoes pre-5th century AH Muslim thought, with few exceptions like al-Juwayni.

Key Terms
قياس التمثيل
مسالك العلة
الحد الأوسط

D) The analogy must be from the present to the absent.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya defends analogical reasoning as capable of certainty when the common attribute is established as a cause through inductive methods.

The Syllogism

Tamthil as the Foundation of Categorical Syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya argues tamthil is the foundation of shumul and both are literal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that analogical reasoning is the foundation of categorical syllogism and evaluate the debate over literal vs metaphorical usage.

Core Idea
For Ibn Taymiyya, categorical syllogism is merely a formalized version of the analogy that grounds its universal premise.

Ibn Taymiyya goes further to argue that analogical reasoning is actually the foundation of categorical syllogism. He states that universal premises are derived from particular analogies, and that the same paths of causation apply to both. He also rejects the views of al-Ghazali and Ibn Hazm that one is literal and the other metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Trace the derivation of universal premises
Example: Knowing that 'this fire burns' leads by analogy that 'all fire burns'. The universal 'every fire burns' comes from analogical induction.
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'إذا عرفنا أن هذه النار محرقة، علمنا أن النار الغائبة محرقة لأنها مثلها'.
2
See how the masalik al-illa apply to both
The same methods that establish causation in tamthil also validate the major premise in shumul. If they yield certainty in tamthil, the major premise is certain; if only probability, then the major premise is probable.
3
Analyze the relationship: foundation vs derived
Ibn Taymiyya says explicitly: 'القياس التمثيلي هو أصل للقياس الشمولي' (tamthil is the foundation for shumul). Reason: shumul relies on a universal premise, and the way to arrive at that universal is through tamthil.
4
Evaluate the literal/metaphorical debate
Al-Ghazali and al-Maqdisi said tamthil is literal, shumul metaphorical; Ibn Hazm said the opposite. Ibn Taymiyya says both are literal, differing only in form of inference.
He states: 'بل هو في الإثنين حقيقة، والقياس العقلي يتناولهما جميعاً، وحقيقته في كل منهما واحدة'.

يذكر ابن تيمية أن المناطقة الأرسططاليسيين يمثلون للتمثيل بالمثال المشهور : السماء مؤلفة فتكون محدثة قياساً على الإنسان ثم يوردون على هذا القياس ما يثبت ضعفه . مع أنه لو قيل السماء مؤلفة ، وكل مؤلف محدث ، لأمكن توجيه تلك الاعتراضات إليه بعينها . بل إن ابن تيمية يذهب إلى القول بأن اليقين أقوى في قياس تمثيل منه في قياس الشمول ، لأن الحكم يكون ثابتاً في الأصل ، أي علم ثبوته في بعض الجزئيات والجزئيات هي الحقائق الوحيدة الموجودة في الأعيان . ثم يرى أنه لا يكفي في قياس التمثيل إثبات الحكم في أحد الجزئيين لثبوته في الجزئي الآخر ، لاشتراكهما في أمر لم يقم دليل على استلزامه للحكم ؛ بل لا بد من العلم بأن المشترك بين الجزئيين ، مستلزم للحكم والمشترك بينهما هو الحد الأوسط . ويسمى عند الأصوليين - المطالبة بتأثير الوصف في الحكم ، إلا أن الاعتراضات تتوجه نحوها فإما أن تمنع الوصف في الأصل ، وإما أن تمنع الحكم في الأصل وإما أن تمنع الوصف في الفرع ، وإما أن تمنع كون الوصف علة في الحكم . وينكر أن يكون الوصف المذكور علة أو دليل علة . فلا بد أن يلجأ إلى دليل من سبر وتقسيم أو مناسبة أو دوران . وهذه المسالك كافية أن تؤدي إلى اليقين تماماً . وبهذا يمتاز قياس الشاهد على الغائب عن قياس الشمول . وصل ابن تيمية إذن إلى النتيجة التي وصل إليها المحدثون ، من أن قياس التمثيل إذا ما استند على قوانين الإستقراء العلي - وهي التي كان يسميها المسلمون مسالك العلة - أدى إلى نتائج علمية صحيحة . ولكن ابن تيمية يقول إن هذه المسالك التي دلت على أن الوصف المشترك وهو العلة أو دليلها مستلزم للحكم ، تدل أيضاً في قياس الشمول على « أن مستلزم الحد الأوسط للأكبر ، وهو الدال على صحة المقدمة الكبرى : فإن أثبت العلة كان برهان له ، وإن أثبت دليلها كان برهان دلالة ، وإن لم تفد العلم بل أفادت الظن ، فكذلك المقدمة الكبرى في ذلك القياس لا تكون إلا ظنية » . فقياس التمثيل إذن يكاد يكون أقوى من قياس الشمول ، بل إن ابن تيمية يعتبره أيضاً أصلاً لقياس الشمول « القياس التمثيلي هو أصل للقياس الشمولي » والسبب في هذا أن قياس الشمول يستند على قضية كلية وابن تيمية يرى أن طريق التوصل إلى هذه القضية الكلية هو قياس التمثيل ، فيرى أن العلم بالقضية الكلية لا بد له من سبب ، فإن عرفوها باعتبار الغائب بالشاهد ، وأن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ، كما إذا عرفنا أن هذه النار محرقة ، علمنا أن النار الغائبة محرقة لأنها مثلها ، وحكم الشيء حكم مثله ـ وهذا قياس تمثيل . وفي موضع آخر يذكر أن النفس تحكم بأن ذلك الغائب مثل ذلك الشاهد أو أنه يساويه في السبب الموجب له . ويسميه قياس التمثيل والتعليل ويذكر أنه مما يحتج به الفقهاء في إثبات الأحكام الشرعية . ويرى ابن تيمية أن نظار المسلمين استخدموا قياس التمثيل في العقليات ، كما استخدم الفقهاء قياس الشمول في الفقهيات ، لأنه إذا ثبت أن الوصف مستلزم للحكم ، كان هذا دليلاً في جميع العلوم . وحيث لا يستدل بالقياس التمثيلي ، لا يستدل بالقياس الشمولي ولا يعتبر القياس حقيقة في قياس التمثيل ، مجازاً في قياس الشمول ، كما يرى الغزالي والمقدسي - ولا بالعكس كما يرى ابن حزم - بل هو في الإثنين حقيقة ، والقياس العقلي يتناولهما جميعاً ، وحقيقته في كل منهما واحدة ، والخلاف في صورة الإستدلال . ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن ابن تيمية في اعتباره لقياس الغائب على الشاهد موصلاً إلى اليقين ، يردد الفكرة التي أخذ بها نظار المسلمين قبل القرن الخامس . فقد اعتبر جميع النظار - ما عدا مفكرين قلائل كأبي المعالي كما
Ibn Taymiyya mentions that the Aristotelian logicians present for analogical reasoning (tamthil) the famous example: the heavens are composed, so they are temporally originated, by analogy to man. Then they raise against this syllogism what proves its weakness. However, if one had said 'the heavens are composed, and everything composed is temporally originated,' those same objections would also be directed at it. Rather, Ibn Taymiyya goes so far as to say that certainty is stronger in analogical reasoning than in categorical syllogism, because the judgment is established in the original, i.e., knowledge of its existence is in some particulars, and particulars are the only realities existing in concrete individuals. Then he holds that in analogical reasoning it is not sufficient to prove the judgment in one of the two particulars because it is established in the other particular, due to their sharing in a matter for which no evidence exists that it necessitates the judgment; rather, it is necessary to know that the common feature between the two particulars necessitates the judgment, and the common feature between them is the middle term. And among the legal theorists it is called 'demanding the effectiveness of the attribute on the judgment,' except that objections are directed at it: either they prevent the attribute in the original, or they prevent the judgment in the original, or they prevent the attribute in the branch, or they prevent the attribute being the cause of the judgment. And he denies that the mentioned attribute is a cause (illa) or evidence of a cause. So one must resort to evidence from [methods like] investigation and division (sabr wa taqsim), or suitability (munasaba), or concomitance (dawaran). And these paths are sufficient to lead to certainty completely. And by this, the syllogism of the witnessed upon the absent is distinguished from categorical syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya thus arrived at the conclusion reached by the moderns, that analogical reasoning, if it relies on the higher laws of induction – which the Muslims used to call 'paths of the cause' – leads to correct scientific results. But Ibn Taymiyya says: these paths which have indicated that the common attribute, which is the cause or its evidence, necessitates the judgment, also indicate in categorical syllogism that 'the necessitation of the middle term for the major term, which indicates the correctness of the major premise: if it establishes the cause, it is a demonstration (burhan) of it, and if it establishes its evidence, it is a demonstrative proof (burhan dalaala), and if it does not provide knowledge but provides conjecture, then likewise the major premise in that syllogism is nothing but conjectural.' So analogical reasoning is almost stronger than categorical syllogism, rather Ibn Taymiyya also considers it a foundation for categorical syllogism: 'Analogical reasoning is the foundation for categorical syllogism.' The reason is that categorical syllogism is based on a universal proposition, and Ibn Taymiyya holds that the way to arrive at this universal proposition is analogical reasoning. He holds that knowledge of the universal proposition must have a cause; for if they know it by considering the absent in light of the present, and that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like – just as when we know that this fire is burning, we know that the absent fire is burning because it is like it, and the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like – this is analogical reasoning. In another place, he mentions that the soul judges that the absent is like the present or that it equals it in the cause that necessitates it. And he calls it analogical reasoning and causal reasoning and mentions that it is what the jurists use in establishing legal rulings. Ibn Taymiyya holds that the Muslim speculative theologians used analogical reasoning in intellectual matters, just as the jurists used categorical syllogism in legal matters, because if it is established that the attribute necessitates the judgment, this is evidence in all sciences. And where analogical reasoning is not used as evidence, categorical syllogism is not used as evidence. And he does not consider reasoning to be literal in analogical reasoning and metaphorical in categorical syllogism, as al-Ghazali and al-Maqdisi hold – nor the opposite, as Ibn Hazm holds – rather it is literal in both, and rational reasoning encompasses both, and its reality in each is one, and the difference is in the form of inference. It should be noted that Ibn Taymiyya, in his consideration of the syllogism of the absent upon the present as yielding certainty, echoes the idea adopted by Muslim speculative theologians before the fifth century [AH]. For all the speculative theologians considered – except for a few thinkers like Abu al-Ma'ali, as...
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critical evaluation of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) versus categorical syllogism (qiyas al-shumul). The author begins by noting that Aristotelians criticize analogical reasoning using the example of the heavens being composite like humans, but Ibn Taymiyya points out that the same objections apply if the argument were framed as a categorical syllogism. He then makes his central claim: certainty is stronger in analogical reasoning because its judgment is directly known in particular instances, which are the only real existents. For a valid analogy, one must know that the common feature (al-wasf al-mushtarak) – which is the middle term – necessitates the judgment. This parallels the legal theorists' requirement that the attribute be effective in the judgment, with possible objections concerning the attribute in the original or branch, the judgment, or the attribute's causality. Ibn Taymiyya insists that the attribute alone is not enough; one must have evidence from methods like investigation and division (sabr wa taqsim), suitability (munasaba), or concomitance (dawaran) – these are the 'paths of the cause' (masalik al-illa) that lead to certainty. Thus, the syllogism of the witnessed upon the absent (qiyas al-shahid 'ala al-gha'ib) is distinguished from categorical syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya then argues that these same paths also apply in categorical syllogism to verify that the middle term necessitates the major, and if they yield only probability, the major premise too is probable. Hence, analogical reasoning is nearly stronger and is actually the foundation of categorical syllogism, because universal premises are derived from induction via analogy. For example, knowing that 'this fire burns' leads, by analogy of similarity, to the universal rule that 'all fire burns.' Ibn Taymiyya notes that both Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) and jurists (fuqaha) use these forms interchangeably, and he rejects claims that one form is literal and the other metaphorical – both are equally valid in their reality, differing only in the form of inference. Finally, the passage observes that Ibn Taymiyya's view on the certainty of analogical reasoning echoes pre-5th century AH Muslim thought, with few exceptions like al-Juwayni.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the relationship between analogical reasoning (tamthil) and categorical syllogism (shumul)?
A) Tamthil is metaphorical, shumul is literal.
B) Shumul is the foundation of tamthil.
C) Tamthil is the foundation of shumul.
D) They are unrelated and operate independently.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya inverts the priority: analogical reasoning is the foundation from which categorical syllogisms derive their universal premises.
