The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya on the Epistemic Priority of Analogical Reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya's argument for analogical reasoning as foundational

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) is epistemically prior to and foundational for universal syllogism (qiyas al-shumul), and evaluate the subsequent debate on its certainty.

Core Idea
Analogical reasoning, not universal syllogism, is the true source of certainty for Ibn Taymiyya.

This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's radical revaluation of analogical reasoning. Against the dominant tradition that treats it as merely conjectural, he argues that analogical reasoning is the very foundation of universal syllogism and can yield certain knowledge. The passage also presents later reactions, including a fierce defense by Muhibb al-Din Abd al-Shakur and criticisms by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of qiyas
From the passage, distinguish between qiyas al-tamthil (analogical reasoning) and qiyas al-shumul (universal syllogism). Note their definitions and how they differ in form.
Tamthil moves from particular to particular; shumul moves from universal to particular.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's central thesis
Find the sentence: 'Analogical reasoning is the foundation of universal syllogism.' What does Ibn Taymiyya mean by this?
3
Analyze his reasoning
Ibn Taymiyya argues that universal premises (qadaya kulliyya) cannot be known without analogical inference from particulars. Use the fire example: observing one fire burning leads to the universal that all fires burn. Trace this reasoning.
The principle 'judgment of a thing is judgment of its like' underlies this.
4
Understand his position on literal vs metaphorical usage
Ibn Taymiyya rejects al-Ghazali's view that 'qiyas' is literal for tamthil and metaphorical for shumul, and Ibn Hazm's opposite view. He says both are literal. Why is this significant?
This elevates the epistemic status of tamthil to equal that of shumul.
5
Examine the later debate
Muhibb al-Din Abd al-Shakur defends analogy as yielding certainty if premises are decisive, while al-Tusi argues it does not guarantee certainty. Identify their key points.
Al-Tusi's objection: the common attribute may not be the actual cause in the branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن لم تفد العلم بل أفادت الظن ، فكذلك المقدمة الكبرى في ذلك القياس لا تكون إلا ظنية.
فقياس التمثيل إذن يكاد يكون أقوى من قياس الشمول ، بل إن ابن تيمية يعتبره أيضاً أصلاً لقياس الشمول « القياس التمثيلي هو أصل للقياس الشمولي » والسبب في هذا أن قياس الشمول يستند على قضية كلية وابن تيمية يرى أن طريق التوصل إلى هذه القضية الكلية هو قياس التمثيل ، فيرى أن العلم بالقضية الكلية لا بد له من سبب ، فإن عرفوها باعتبار الغائب بالشاهد ، وأن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ، كما إذا عرفنا أن هذه النار محرقة ، علمنا أن النار الغائبة محرقة لأنها مثلها ، وحكم الشيء حكم مثله ـ وهذا قياس تمثيل.
وفي موضع آخر يذكر أن النفس تحكم بأن ذلك الغائب مثل ذلك الشاهد أو أنه يساويه في السبب الموجب له . ويسميه قياس التمثيل والتعليل ويذكر أنه مما يحتج به الفقهاء في إثبات الأحكام الشرعية.
ويرى ابن تيمية أن نظار المسلمين استخدموا قياس التمثيل في العقليات ، كما استخدم الفقهاء قياس الشمول في الفقهيات ، لأنه إذا ثبت أن الوصف مستلزم للحكم ، كان هذا دليلاً في جميع العلوم . وحيث لا يستدل بالقياس التمثيلي ، لا يستدل بالقياس الشمولي ولا يعتبر القياس حقيقة في قياس التمثيل ، مجازاً في قياس الشمول ، كما يرى الغزالي والمقدسي - ولا بالعكس كما يرى ابن حزم - بل هو في الإثنين حقيقة ، والقياس العقلي يتناولهما جميعاً ، وحقيقته في كل منهما واحدة ، والخلاف في صورة الإستدلال.
ينبغي أن نلاحظ أن ابن تيمية في اعتباره لقياس الغائب على الشاهد موصلاً إلى اليقين ، يردد الفكرة التي أخذ بها نظار المسلمين قبل القرن الخامس . فقد اعتبر جميع النظار - ما عدا مفكرين قلائل كأبي المعالي كما سيأتي بعد ـ قياس الغائب على الشاهد موصلاً إلى اليقين . وسلكوا هذا الطريق في إثبات مطالبهم التي يطلب فيها اليقين ويقول إمام الحرمين « رتب أئمتنا أدلة العقول ترتيباً ننقله ثم نبين فساده » ثم يعرض هذه الطرق عند المتقدمين وينكر ـ كما ذكرنا من قبل ـ ما ذهب إليه المسلمون من أن قياس الغائب على الشاهد طريق يقيني . ونستطيع أن نجد أنصاراً لفكرة يقينية قياس الغائب على الشاهد عند المتأخرين ، ولدى منطقي ممتاز هو صاحب شرح سلم بحر العلوم ـ محب الدين عبد الشكور ـ فهو ينكر إنكاراً باتاً أن يكون التمثيل حجة ضعيفة أو يفيد الظن . وينسب القول بهذا إلى سوء فهم الشيخ ابن سينا واتباعه من متفلسفة المناطقة ثم يقول « إن النصير الطوسي قد انتهى في هذا الأمر حد البلادة ، وظن أنه لا يلزم منه شيء » ويقرر أن التمثيل راجع إلى القياس فإن كانت مقدماته قطعية ، تورث القطع كالقياس المنطقي ، ويرى أنه لا يمكن لإنسان كائناً من كان أن يشك في أنه « إذا ثبت أن حكم الجزئي معلول لعلة قطعاً ، وهي موجودة في جزئي آخر قطعاً ، في أن يثبت ذلك الحكم في ذلك الجزئي قطعاً ، لا سيما إذا علم قطعاً أن العلة انما وضعت علة ليتعدى الحكم بها » أما إذا كانت المقدمات ظنية ، فإن قياس التمثيل يورث الظن . أما أن أقيسة الفقهاء ظنية . فليس يعود ذلك إلى ضعف في صورة التمثيل نفسه « ولكن لأن الأغلب في مقدماته الظن ». ويورد بعد ذلك عبارة النصير الطوسي في نقد التمثيل بأنه « لا يفيد القطع لأن الجامع يكون علة للحكم في الأصل لكونه أصلاً . ثم دون الفرع وربما ينقسم إلى قسمين يكون أحدهما علة دون الثاني . وقد اختص الأصل بالأول ثم إن صح كون الجامع علة في الفرع ، كان الاستدلال به برهاناً .
If it does not yield knowledge but rather yields conjecture, then similarly the major premise in that syllogism can only be conjectural. Therefore, analogical reasoning is almost stronger than universal syllogism. Indeed, Ibn Taymiyya considers it also the foundation of universal syllogism: 'Analogical reasoning is the foundation of universal syllogism.' The reason for this is that universal syllogism relies on a universal proposition, and Ibn Taymiyya holds that the way to arrive at this universal proposition is analogical reasoning. He holds that knowledge of a universal proposition must have a cause. If they know it by considering the unseen in light of the seen, and that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like – as when we know that this fire is burning, we know that the unseen fire is burning because it is like it, and the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like – this is analogical reasoning. In another place, he mentions that the soul judges that the unseen is like the seen or that it equals it in the cause necessitating the judgment. He calls it analogical reasoning and reasoning by cause, and mentions that jurists use it to establish legal rulings. Ibn Taymiyya holds that Muslim speculative theologians used analogical reasoning in rational matters, just as jurists used universal syllogism in jurisprudential matters, because if it is established that the descriptive attribute entails the ruling, this is evidence in all sciences. Where analogical reasoning is not used, universal syllogism is not used. And he does not consider 'syllogism' to be used literally for analogical reasoning and metaphorically for universal syllogism, as al-Ghazali and al-Maqdisi hold – nor the opposite as Ibn Hazm holds – rather it is literal in both. Rational syllogism encompasses both, and its reality in each is one; the disagreement is only in the form of inference. We should note that Ibn Taymiyya, in considering analogy from the unseen to the seen as leading to certainty, repeats the idea adopted by Muslim thinkers before the fifth century. All thinkers – except a few like Abu al-Ma'ali as will come later – considered analogy from the unseen to the seen as leading to certainty. They followed this path in establishing their claims where certainty is sought. Imam al-Haramayn says: 'Our imams arranged the proofs of the intellect in an order that we will transmit and then show its corruption.' Then he presents these methods of the earlier scholars and denies – as we mentioned before – what the Muslims held that analogy from the unseen to the seen is a certain path. We can find supporters of the idea of certainty of analogy from the unseen to the seen among later scholars, including an eminent logician, the author of Sharh Sullam Bahr al-Ulum – Muhibb al-Din Abd al-Shakur. He absolutely denies that analogical reasoning is a weak proof or that it yields conjecture. He attributes this claim to a misunderstanding by Ibn Sina and his followers among the philosopher-logicians. Then he says: 'Nasir al-Din al-Tusi has reached the utmost dullness on this matter, thinking that nothing follows from it.' He affirms that analogical reasoning reduces to syllogism: if its premises are decisive, it yields certainty like logical syllogism. He holds that no human being, whoever they may be, can doubt that 'if it is established that the judgment of a particular is necessarily caused by a cause, and that cause exists in another particular necessarily, then that judgment is established in that particular necessarily – especially if it is known necessarily that the cause is only placed as a cause so that the judgment extends by it.' As for when the premises are conjectural, then analogical reasoning yields conjecture. As for jurists' analogies being conjectural, that is not due to weakness in the form of analogical reasoning itself, 'but because most of its premises are conjectural.' Then he quotes the statement of Nasir al-Din al-Tusi in criticizing analogical reasoning: 'It does not yield certainty because the common attribute is a cause for the judgment in the original because it is an original, then it may be less than the branch, and perhaps it divides into two parts, one of which is a cause and the other not. The original is particularized by the first. Then if it is valid that the common attribute is a cause in the branch, then the inference by it is a demonstration.'
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage examines Ibn Taymiyya's position on the epistemic value of analogical reasoning (qiyas al-tamthil) and its relationship to universal syllogism (qiyas al-shumul). The author begins by stating a conditional: if analogical reasoning yields only conjecture (zann) rather than certain knowledge ('ilm), then the major premise in any syllogism based on it can only be conjectural. However, Ibn Taymiyya argues that analogical reasoning is almost stronger than universal syllogism, even considering it the foundation of universal syllogism. The reason is that universal syllogism depends on a universal proposition, and the only way to arrive at that universal is through analogical reasoning from particulars. He gives the example of fire: knowing that this particular fire burns, we infer that any unseen fire burns because it is similar, and the rule is that the judgment of a thing applies to its like. This is the core of analogical reasoning. Ibn Taymiyya further notes that the soul naturally judges similarity of cause between the seen and the unseen. He calls this 'analogical reasoning and reasoning by cause' (qiyas al-tamthil wa al-ta'lil) and states that jurists use it to prove legal rulings. He then observes that Muslim theologians used analogical reasoning in rational matters, while jurists used universal syllogism in legal matters, because if a descriptive attribute is found to entail a ruling, that becomes evidence in all sciences. Significantly, Ibn Taymiyya rejects the view of al-Ghazali and al-Maqdisi that 'syllogism' is literal for analogical reasoning and metaphorical for universal syllogism, as well as Ibn Hazm's opposite view. For Ibn Taymiyya, 'syllogism' is literal in both cases; the rational syllogism encompasses both forms, and its reality is one. The disagreement is only about the form of inference. The author then notes that Ibn Taymiyya's affirmation of analogical reasoning as leading to certainty repeats the position of earlier Muslim thinkers before the 5th century AH, with few exceptions like Abu al-Ma'ali (Imam al-Haramayn). Imam al-Haramayn himself criticized that position, claiming he would transmit and then show its corruption. However, the author finds supporters even among later scholars, notably Muhibb al-Din Abd al-Shakur, the commentator on al-Sullam. He strongly denies that analogical reasoning is weak or yields only conjecture, attributing that view to a misunderstanding of Ibn Sina and his followers. He attacks Nasir al-Din al-Tusi for dullness on this issue. He asserts that analogical reasoning reduces to syllogism: if its premises are decisive (qat'iyya), it yields certainty like any logical syllogism. He gives a formal statement: if a particular judgment is necessarily caused by a cause, and that cause exists in another particular, then the judgment applies to the other particular—especially if it is known that the cause is placed to extend the judgment. If the premises are conjectural, then the result is conjecture. The conjectural nature of jurists' analogies, he clarifies, is not due to the form of analogical reasoning but because most of their premises are conjectural. Finally, the author quotes al-Tusi's criticism: analogical reasoning does not yield certainty because the common attribute may not be the actual cause in the branch; it might be divided into two parts, only one of which is the cause. If it can be shown that the common attribute is indeed the cause in the branch, then the inference becomes a demonstration (burhan). Thus, the passage comprehensively presents the debate on the epistemic status of analogy in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
قياس التمثيل
قياس الشمول
اليقين
قضية كلية
الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ibn Taymiyya's Critical Nuance: Acknowledging Syllogistic Validity but Rejecting Certainty
Author begins Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the claim that syllogism yields knowledge of assertions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the claim that syllogism yields certainty from a wholesale rejection of syllogistic reasoning, using his own acknowledgment of the first figure's validity.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya critiques not the syllogism itself, but its claim to yield certainty.

After dismantling earlier claims about definition and classification, Ibn Taymiyya now confronts the heart of Aristotelian logic: the syllogism. But here his critique takes a surprising turn. He does not deny that a valid syllogism yields a correct conclusion; he even concedes the clarity of the first figure. Yet he insists that this formal validity does not grant the syllogism any privileged epistemological status. The logicians' methods are overcomplicated and do not deliver the 'verification' (tahqiq) they promise. Let us follow his delicate argument and see what exactly he accepts and what he rejects.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's overall stance
Read the passage and find where Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states his position on the syllogism's ability to produce a conclusion. Notice that he says the fact that a two-premise syllogism yields a conclusion 'is correct in itself' (huwa sahih fi nafsih).
This is a crucial concession that many critics overlook.
2
Locate his critique of the logicians' method
Find where Ibn Taymiyya accuses the logicians of making the path 'rugged' (wa'r) and 'lengthening the ways' (tawala al-turuq). He says the truth in the syllogism does not need them.
This is the core of his refinement: the logicians have obscured rather than clarified.
3
Understand the target of the critique
Determine what exactly Ibn Taymiyya is attacking. It is not the syllogism's formal validity, but the claim that the syllogism, especially the first figure, yields certain knowledge (al-ilm bi al-tasdiqat) in the way logicians describe.
The target is epistemological: the logicians' assertion that their method provides certainty where simpler reasoning suffices.
4
Recognize the incomplete nature of the argument
The passage cuts off mid-sentence. Note that Ibn Taymiyya's full critique would extend further, but here he introduces the idea that there is 'no scientific benefit' (laysa thamma fa'ida 'ilmiyya) in the logicians' approach. The argument is likely to develop into a discussion of how real knowledge comes from other sources.
Be aware that the text is truncated; do not assume the argument ends here.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the foundation and form of syllogism
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the claim that syllogism is necessary for knowledge and its neglect of material

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the syllogism's necessity and its neglect of material content, and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
Syllogistic form alone cannot guarantee knowledge; material implication and truth of premises are what matter.

Ibn Taymiyya, the great Hanbali thinker, launched a profound critique of Aristotelian logic that remains relevant today. In this passage, he attacks two central claims of the logicians: that syllogism is necessary for certain knowledge, and that its formal rules are sufficient to evaluate proofs. He argues that proof can be a single premise, that knowledge of particulars is clearer than universals, and that logicians' obsession with form ignores the real basis of inference—material implication. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claims
The logicians claim syllogism has a fixed minimum of premises and yields only universal knowledge; Ibn Taymiyya attacks both.
2
Extract the rejection of fixed limits
Ibn Taymiyya says proof can be a single premise and that people differ in capacity, so no universal standard for premises exists.
3
Unpack the particular vs universal argument
He argues that knowledge of particulars is more precise and possible without comprehensive syllogism, contrary to logicians' view.
Look for his statement: 'Knowledge of concrete individuals may be clearer than knowledge of universals.'
4
Analyze the formal neglect critique
Logicians only examine the form (surah) of the syllogism, completely neglecting the material (maddah)—i.e., truth of premises and real implication.
5
Examine material implication as alternative
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that real inference relies on recognizing necessary entailment (luzum madi), not syllogistic shape.
Even without uttering 'implcation', one can infer by knowing that one thing necessitates another.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن هنا يؤكده بصورة جازمة، ويرى أن الدليل قد يكون مقدمة واحدة، متى علمناها علمنا المطلوب، وقد نحتاج إلى أكثر، وأنه ليس هناك حد مقدر يتساوى فيه جميع الناس في جميع المطالب. إنهم يختلفون ويتفاوتون بحسب علم المستدل الطالب بأحوال المطلوب، والدليل، ولوازم ذلك وملزوماته. ثم يلجأ ابن تيمية إلى نقد القياس في أساسه. إنه عند المناطقة لا يوصل إلا إلى العلم بأمور كلية ولا يؤدي إلى العلم بشيء معين أي الجزئيات. يرد ابن تيمية ويقول إن العلم بالجزئيات هو أدق وأيسر وأكثر برهنة وإقامة للعلم. والعلم بجزئياتها ممكن بدون «القياس الشمولي». يقول «إن العلم بالمعينات قد يكون أبين من العلم بالكليات». وينقد ابن تيمية الجانب الصوري من القياس، ويرى أن المناطقة يقتصرون على التمسك بهذا الجانب مهملين المادة إهمالاً تاماً، ولا يهتمون بما فيها من صحة وفساد. ويرى ابن تيمية أن المطلوب هو العلم، والطريق إليه هو الدليل، فما عرف دليل مطلوبه، عرف مطلوبه، سواء نظمه في صورة قياس أم لم ينظمه. ومن لم يعرف دليله، لم ينفعه قياس أو غير قياس. إن من الجهل أن يقال إننا بالقياس نعرف صحيح الأدلة من فاسدها. إنه ليس في قواعد القياس إلا شكل الدليل وصورته. ولا يتعرض القياس للمادة بإثبات أو نفي أي لكون الدليل المعين مستلزماً لمدلوله. وليس في القياس ما يبحث في صحة شيء من المقدمات أو عدم صحتها. إنما يبحث فقط في الصورة وحدها. بينما يرى ابن تيمية أن الحقيقة المعتبرة في كل برهان ودليل في العالم هو اللزوم المادي، فمن عرف أن هذا لازم لهذا، استدل بالملزوم على اللازم، وإن لم يكن يذكر لفظ اللزوم، ولا تصور معناه، بل من عرف أن كذا لا بد له من كذا أو أنه إذا كان كذا... فقد علم اللزوم.
But here he affirms it decisively, and he holds that the proof may be a single premise; when we know it, we know the required conclusion, and we may need more, and that there is no fixed limit at which all people are equal in all inquiries. They differ and vary according to the knowledge of the seeker, the inquirer, of the states of the required conclusion, the proof, and its implications and necessitants. Then Ibn Taymiyya proceeds to criticize syllogism in its foundation. According to the logicians, it leads only to knowledge of universal matters and does not lead to knowledge of any specific thing, i.e., particulars. Ibn Taymiyya responds and says that knowledge of particulars is more precise, easier, and more demonstrative and establisher of knowledge. And knowledge of their particulars is possible without 'the comprehensive syllogism'. He says: 'Knowledge of concrete individuals may be clearer than knowledge of universals.' And Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the formal aspect of syllogism, holding that the logicians restrict themselves to clinging to this aspect, completely neglecting the material, and they do not care about its correctness or corruption. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that what is sought is knowledge, and the path to it is the proof. Whoever knows the proof of his sought conclusion knows his sought conclusion, whether he arranges it in the form of a syllogism or not. And whoever does not know his proof, no syllogism or non-syllogism benefits him. It is ignorance to say that through syllogism we know correct proofs from corrupt ones. There is in the rules of syllogism only the form and shape of the proof. And the syllogism does not deal with the material by affirmation or negation, i.e., whether a particular proof entails its implication. And there is nothing in syllogism that examines the correctness of any premises or their incorrectness. It only examines the form alone. Whereas Ibn Taymiyya holds that the real consideration in every demonstration and proof in the world is material implication. So whoever knows that this entails that, infers from the necessitant to the necessary consequent, even if he does not utter the term implication, nor conceive its meaning. Rather, whoever knows that such and such cannot be without such and such, or that if it is such and such... then he has known the implication.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the syllogism as defined by logicians. It is a critical refinement, challenging the epistemological necessity and formal preoccupation of Greek logic. Ibn Taymiyya begins by asserting that proof (dalil) can be a single premise, rejecting the logicians' requirement for a minimum of two premises in a syllogism. He emphasizes that people differ in their intellectual capacities and prior knowledge, so there is no universal standard for the number of premises needed. Then he attacks the core claim that syllogism only yields universal knowledge, not knowledge of particulars. Ibn Taymiyya counters that knowledge of particulars is actually more precise and easier, and it can be attained without 'comprehensive syllogism' (al-qiyas al-shumuli). He gives the example that knowledge of concrete individuals may be clearer than universals. Next, he criticizes the logicians for focusing only on the formal structure (surah) of the syllogism while completely neglecting the material (maddah) — i.e., the actual content and truth of the premises. For Ibn Taymiyya, the real requirement is knowledge of the proof itself, not its arrangement in syllogistic form. A person who knows the proof knows the conclusion regardless of formal organization. Conversely, arranging a syllogism without knowing the proof is useless. He labels as ignorance the claim that syllogism can distinguish correct proofs from corrupt ones, because syllogistic rules only deal with form, not with the truth of premises or the material implication between them. Knowledge of implication (luzum madi) is the true foundation of reasoning: one who recognizes that one thing necessarily implies another can infer correctly without any formal syllogism. This critique undermines the logicians' claim that syllogism is indispensable for certain knowledge.

Key Terms
الدليل
القياس الشمولي
اللزوم المادي
الصورة
المادة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that syllogistic form is neither necessary nor sufficient for knowledge; what matters is the material implication between a proof and its conclusion.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Ibn Taymiyya's critique: syllogism as dispensible mental exercise
Ibn Taymiyya gives an example and critiques syllogism as unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that Aristotelian syllogism is a mental exercise with no practical benefit and identify his use of material implication.

Core Idea
Syllogism is valid but not needed; material implication and Muslim theology are sufficient.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that syllogistic form is necessary for certain knowledge. Using the example of everything in existence as a sign of God, he shows that inference by material implication (malzum-lazim) works without syllogistic structure. He then concludes that syllogism is merely a mental exercise, and Islamic knowledge is independent of Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example and the inference
Everything in existence is a sign of God; therefore, its existence implies the Creator. Note that this is not a syllogism but an immediate implication.
2
Distinguish material implication from syllogistic form
Ibn Taymiyya says one can reason without setting up two premises. The inference uses the relation of malzum (necessitant) and lazim (necessary consequent).
3
Locate the claim about syllogism
He calls the Aristotelian syllogism a 'mental exercise' (riyada 'aqliyya) with no practical benefit, meaning it adds nothing to the material.
4
Understand his conclusion
The syllogism in form is correct, but no knowledge is gained by it that could not be gained otherwise. Certainty can be attained without it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعطي ابن تيمية مثالاً على هذا: إن من يعرف أن كل ما في الوجود فهو آية الله، فإنه مفتقر إلى الله، محتاج إليه، لا بد له منه، فيلزم من وجوده وجود الصانع. فهنا استدلال بالملزوم على اللازم توصل منها الإنسان إلى علاقة الإستلزام، بدون أن يضع هذا الدليل في صورة قياس أرسططاليسي. ولا أن يلتزم مقدمتين يستنتج منهما نتيجة. حقاً قد يستخدم القياس الأرسططاليسي «من يناسب عادته، لا لكون العلم بالمطلوب متوقفاً عليه مطلقاً». فالقياس المنطقي «رياضة عقلية» لا فائدة له عملية. «وإن شريعة الإسلام ومعرفتها ليست موقوفة على شيء تتعلم من غير المسلمين إطلاقاً، وإن كان طريقاً صحيحاً». وينتهي ابن تيمية من نقده للقياس في صورته، إلى أن القياس في صورته صحيح، ولكن لا يستفاد به أي علم في الوجود. وإن اليقين الذي نصل إليه به، فقد نصل إليه بدونه، بل قد يحصل بغيره من الطريق، يقين، لا يحصل عليه به. فإذا كانت مواده معلومة يقينية، فإننا نصل إلى اليقين، ولا ينقد صحتها صورة القياس سواء أكان اقتراناً «قياس التداخل» أو استثنائياً في صورتيه الشرطية المتصلة أو المنفصلة «قياس التلازم، أو التقسيم». أما الأول: فهو قياس التداخل، وقد عرفه المسلمون بأنفسهم ولم يستخدموه. وأما الثاني: وهو الشرطي المتصل عند المناطقة فقد عرفه نظار المسلمين بأنفسهم: إنه عند المناطقة يحدد بأنه عملية عقلية شروطها هي: استثناء عين المقدم ينتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم. وعند نظار المسلمين «وجود الملزوم يقتضي وجود اللازم، وانتفاء اللازم يقتضي انتفاء الملزوم». وهذا هو طريق أبي بكر الباقلاني المشهور. ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذا الطريق مع اختصاره، فإنه يشمل جميع أنواع الأدلة.
And Ibn Taymiyya gives an example of this: Whoever knows that everything in existence is a sign of God, then it is dependent on God, in need of Him, cannot do without Him, so from its existence follows the existence of the Creator. Here is inference by the necessitant from the necessary consequent; one arrives at the relation of implication, without placing this proof in the form of an Aristotelian syllogism. Nor does he commit himself to two premises from which he deduces a conclusion. Indeed, the Aristotelian syllogism may be used 'by whoever is accustomed to it, not because knowledge of the sought conclusion depends on it absolutely.' So logical syllogism is a 'mental exercise' with no practical benefit. 'And the Islamic Sharia and knowledge of it are in no way dependent on anything learned from non-Muslims at all, even if it is a correct path.' And Ibn Taymiyya concludes from his critique of syllogism in its form that the syllogism in its form is correct, but no knowledge in existence is gained by it. And the certainty we attain through it, we might attain without it; indeed, by other paths certainty may be attained that is not attained through it. So if its materials are known with certainty, we attain certainty, and the form of the syllogism does not critique their correctness, whether it is conjunctive (syllogism of overlap) or exceptive in its two forms: conditional conjunctive or disjunctive (syllogism of implication or division). As for the first: it is the syllogism of overlap, and it was known by the Muslims themselves and they did not use it. As for the second: it is the conditional conjunctive according to the logicians; it was known by the Muslim theologians themselves: According to the logicians, it is defined as a mental operation whose conditions are: taking the antecedent itself yields the consequent itself, and taking the contradictory of the consequent yields the contradictory of the antecedent. According to the Muslim theologians: 'The existence of the necessitant necessitates the existence of the necessary consequent, and the non-existence of the necessary consequent necessitates the non-existence of the necessitant.' This is the well-known path of Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that this path, despite its brevity, encompasses all types of proofs.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya provides a concrete example and draws conclusions about the nature of syllogism. He illustrates reasoning by material implication (luzum) without syllogistic form: everything in existence is a sign (ayah) of God, thus it is dependent on God and requires a Creator. This inference uses the relation of necessitant and necessary consequent (malzum and lazim) without formal syllogistic structure. He argues that Aristotelian syllogism is merely a mental exercise (riyada ‘aqliyya) with no practical benefit. Islamic law and knowledge do not depend on it, even if it is a correct method. Then he summarizes his critique: the syllogism in its form is logically valid, but it does not produce any knowledge beyond what is already known from the material. Certainty can be attained without it, and sometimes certainty is only attained through non-syllogistic paths. He then discusses the two main types of syllogism: conjunctive (iqtirani/ qiyas al-tadakhul) and exceptive (istithna’i), which in its conditional forms (mutassil or munfasil) corresponds to 'syllogism of implication' (talazum) or 'division' (taqsim). He notes that Muslims (meaning Muslim theologians like al-Baqillani) already knew the exceptive conditional syllogism in the form: existence of the necessitant implies existence of the necessary consequent, and non-existence of the necessary consequent implies non-existence of the necessitant — a concise rule covering all proofs. Ibn Taymiyya endorses this as the true method, contrasting it with the logicians' formal version. The passage thus defends the sufficiency of material implication and Muslim theological reasoning, while criticizing the logicians' claim that their formal syllogism is indispensable.

Key Terms
الملزوم
اللازم
رياضة عقلية

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya call the Aristotelian syllogism?
A) The only path to certainty
B) A mental exercise with no practical benefit

D) Identical to material implication

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya critiques syllogism by showing that material implication suffices for certain knowledge and that Greek syllogistic form adds nothing essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Take the proposition 'Every created thing requires a creator.' Write it as a material implication: If X is created, then X has a creator. Then show how from 'the world is created' we infer 'the world has a creator' without using syllogistic form.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Ibn Taymiyya on the two syllogism types and the Muslim theologians' rule
Ibn Taymiyya discusses the two types of syllogism and the Muslim theologians' rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between conjunctive and exceptive syllogism as described by Ibn Taymiyya, and compare the logicians' conditional rules with the Muslim theologians' concise rule of implication.

Core Idea
Muslim theologians already knew exceptive syllogism under a simpler rule: existence of the necessitant implies existence of the necessary consequent.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes two main types of syllogism: conjunctive (iqtirani/tadakhul) and exceptive (istithna'i). He notes that Muslims knew the conjunctive but did not use it, while they used the exceptive in its conditional forms. He then presents the logicians' rule for the conditional exceptive syllogism (affirming antecedent yields consequent; denying consequent yields denying antecedent) and contrasts it with the Muslim theologians' version as formulated by Abu Bakr al-Baqillani: 'The existence of the necessitant necessitates the existence of the necessary consequent, and the non-existence of the necessary consequent necessitates the non-existence of the necessitant.' Ibn Taymiyya holds that this concise rule covers all proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of syllogism
Conjunctive (qiyas al-tadakhul) and exceptive (qiyas al-istithna'i). Note that Ibn Taymiyya says Muslims knew the conjunctive but did not use it.
2
Focus on exceptive syllogism
It has two forms: conditional conjunctive (mutassil) and conditional disjunctive (munfasil). The passage focuses on the conditional conjunctive.
3
Extract the logicians' rule
Presuming the antecedent itself yields the consequent itself; presuming the contradictory of the consequent yields the contradictory of the antecedent. This is modus ponens and modus tollens.
4
Extract the Muslim theologians' rule
The existence of the necessitant (malzum) implies the existence of the necessary consequent (lazim), and the non-existence of the necessary consequent implies the non-existence of the necessitant.
5
Compare the two formulations
They are essentially the same in meaning, but the Muslim version is expressed in terms of malzum and lazim, reflecting material implication.
Ibn Taymiyya holds that this concise rule is sufficient and encompasses all proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعطي ابن تيمية مثالاً على هذا: إن من يعرف أن كل ما في الوجود فهو آية الله، فإنه مفتقر إلى الله، محتاج إليه، لا بد له منه، فيلزم من وجوده وجود الصانع. فهنا استدلال بالملزوم على اللازم توصل منها الإنسان إلى علاقة الإستلزام، بدون أن يضع هذا الدليل في صورة قياس أرسططاليسي. ولا أن يلتزم مقدمتين يستنتج منهما نتيجة. حقاً قد يستخدم القياس الأرسططاليسي «من يناسب عادته، لا لكون العلم بالمطلوب متوقفاً عليه مطلقاً». فالقياس المنطقي «رياضة عقلية» لا فائدة له عملية. «وإن شريعة الإسلام ومعرفتها ليست موقوفة على شيء تتعلم من غير المسلمين إطلاقاً، وإن كان طريقاً صحيحاً». وينتهي ابن تيمية من نقده للقياس في صورته، إلى أن القياس في صورته صحيح، ولكن لا يستفاد به أي علم في الوجود. وإن اليقين الذي نصل إليه به، فقد نصل إليه بدونه، بل قد يحصل بغيره من الطريق، يقين، لا يحصل عليه به. فإذا كانت مواده معلومة يقينية، فإننا نصل إلى اليقين، ولا ينقد صحتها صورة القياس سواء أكان اقتراناً «قياس التداخل» أو استثنائياً في صورتيه الشرطية المتصلة أو المنفصلة «قياس التلازم، أو التقسيم». أما الأول: فهو قياس التداخل، وقد عرفه المسلمون بأنفسهم ولم يستخدموه. وأما الثاني: وهو الشرطي المتصل عند المناطقة فقد عرفه نظار المسلمين بأنفسهم: إنه عند المناطقة يحدد بأنه عملية عقلية شروطها هي: استثناء عين المقدم ينتج عين التالي، واستثناء نقيض التالي ينتج نقيض المقدم. وعند نظار المسلمين «وجود الملزوم يقتضي وجود اللازم، وانتفاء اللازم يقتضي انتفاء الملزوم». وهذا هو طريق أبي بكر الباقلاني المشهور. ويرى ابن تيمية أن هذا الطريق مع اختصاره، فإنه يشمل جميع أنواع الأدلة.
And Ibn Taymiyya gives an example of this: Whoever knows that everything in existence is a sign of God, then it is dependent on God, in need of Him, cannot do without Him, so from its existence follows the existence of the Creator. Here is inference by the necessitant from the necessary consequent; one arrives at the relation of implication, without placing this proof in the form of an Aristotelian syllogism. Nor does he commit himself to two premises from which he deduces a conclusion. Indeed, the Aristotelian syllogism may be used 'by whoever is accustomed to it, not because knowledge of the sought conclusion depends on it absolutely.' So logical syllogism is a 'mental exercise' with no practical benefit. 'And the Islamic Sharia and knowledge of it are in no way dependent on anything learned from non-Muslims at all, even if it is a correct path.' And Ibn Taymiyya concludes from his critique of syllogism in its form that the syllogism in its form is correct, but no knowledge in existence is gained by it. And the certainty we attain through it, we might attain without it; indeed, by other paths certainty may be attained that is not attained through it. So if its materials are known with certainty, we attain certainty, and the form of the syllogism does not critique their correctness, whether it is conjunctive (syllogism of overlap) or exceptive in its two forms: conditional conjunctive or disjunctive (syllogism of implication or division). As for the first: it is the syllogism of overlap, and it was known by the Muslims themselves and they did not use it. As for the second: it is the conditional conjunctive according to the logicians; it was known by the Muslim theologians themselves: According to the logicians, it is defined as a mental operation whose conditions are: taking the antecedent itself yields the consequent itself, and taking the contradictory of the consequent yields the contradictory of the antecedent. According to the Muslim theologians: 'The existence of the necessitant necessitates the existence of the necessary consequent, and the non-existence of the necessary consequent necessitates the non-existence of the necessitant.' This is the well-known path of Abu Bakr al-Baqillani. And Ibn Taymiyya holds that this path, despite its brevity, encompasses all types of proofs.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya provides a concrete example and draws conclusions about the nature of syllogism. He illustrates reasoning by material implication (luzum) without syllogistic form: everything in existence is a sign (ayah) of God, thus it is dependent on God and requires a Creator. This inference uses the relation of necessitant and necessary consequent (malzum and lazim) without formal syllogistic structure. He argues that Aristotelian syllogism is merely a mental exercise (riyada ‘aqliyya) with no practical benefit. Islamic law and knowledge do not depend on it, even if it is a correct method. Then he summarizes his critique: the syllogism in its form is logically valid, but it does not produce any knowledge beyond what is already known from the material. Certainty can be attained without it, and sometimes certainty is only attained through non-syllogistic paths. He then discusses the two main types of syllogism: conjunctive (iqtirani/ qiyas al-tadakhul) and exceptive (istithna’i), which in its conditional forms (mutassil or munfasil) corresponds to 'syllogism of implication' (talazum) or 'division' (taqsim). He notes that Muslims (meaning Muslim theologians like al-Baqillani) already knew the exceptive conditional syllogism in the form: existence of the necessitant implies existence of the necessary consequent, and non-existence of the necessary consequent implies non-existence of the necessitant — a concise rule covering all proofs. Ibn Taymiyya endorses this as the true method, contrasting it with the logicians' formal version. The passage thus defends the sufficiency of material implication and Muslim theological reasoning, while criticizing the logicians' claim that their formal syllogism is indispensable.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الاقتراني
الملزوم
اللازم

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what does the Muslim theologians' rule for exceptive syllogism state?
A) Presuming the antecedent yields the consequent
B) The existence of the necessitant necessitates the existence of the necessary consequent
C) The syllogism must have two premises
D) The syllogism is a mental exercise

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the Muslim theologians' concise rule for implication is equivalent to the logicians' exceptive syllogism and is sufficient for all proofs, making the formal syllogism redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

1. Given the statement: 'If the world is contingent (huduth), then it has a cause.' Using al-Baqillani's rule, show how from 'the world is contingent' you infer 'it has a cause' (affirmation of malzum), and from 'it has no cause' you infer 'the world is not contingent' (negation of lazim). Write it in the malzum-lazim format.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Syllogistic Necessity
Ibn Taymiyya questions necessity of formal syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the necessity of Aristotelian syllogism and contrast it with his alternative inference based on entailment.

Core Idea
Formal syllogism is just a mental exercise; real knowledge comes from understanding entailment.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the claim that Aristotelian syllogism is necessary for certain knowledge. He provides a concrete example from Islamic theology: recognizing that everything in existence is a sign of God leads directly to the conclusion that the Creator exists, without needing to formalize this as a syllogism. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument and understand his epistemological alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's example
Find the specific inference: from creation as a sign of God to the existence of the Creator. The passage states: 'Everything in existence is a sign of God, so from its existence follows the existence of the Maker.'
2
Extract the logical structure of entailment
Identify the ملزوم (necessary condition) and the لازم (necessary consequent). The creation is the ملزوم, and the Creator is the لازم. The inference is: the existence of the ملزوم entails the existence of the لازم.
This form of reasoning is based on the relation of entailment, not on a two-premise syllogism.
3
Recognize the critique of formality
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says that this inference does not require being put into an Aristotelian syllogism nor committing to two premises. He dismisses the formal syllogism as a 'mental exercise' with no practical benefit.
He concedes that some may use it out of habit, but it is not epistemologically essential.
4
Assess the epistemological claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that Islamic law and its knowledge are not dependent on anything learned from non-Muslims, even if that thing is valid. Thus, the logical syllogism is not a necessary tool for Islamic knowledge.
This is a strong epistemological independence claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن تيمية مثالاً على هذا : إن من يعرف أن كل ما في الوجود ، فهو آية الله ، فإنه مفتقر إلى الله ، محتاج إليه ، لا بد له منه ، فيلزم من وجوده ، وجود الصانع . فهنا استدلال بالملزوم على اللزوم توصل منها الإنسان إلى علاقة الإستلزام ، بدون أن يضع هذا الدليل في صورة قياس أرسطاطاليسي . ولا أن يلتزم مقدمتين يستنتج منهما نتيجة . حقاً قد يستخدم القياس الأرسطاطاليسي «من يناسب عادته ، لا لكون العلم بالمطلوب متوقفاً عليه مطلقاً »(۱) . فالقياس المنطقي « رياضة عقلية » لا فائدة له عملية . « وإن شريعة الإسلام ومعرفتها ليست موقوفة على شيء تتعلم من غير المسلمين إطلاقاً ، وإن كان طريقاً صحيحاً »(۲) .
Ibn Taymiyya gives an example of this: Whoever knows that everything in existence is a sign of God, then he is in need of God, dependent on Him, cannot do without Him, so from its existence follows the existence of the Maker. So here is reasoning from the necessary condition to the necessary consequence, by which a person arrives at the relation of entailment, without putting this proof into the form of an Aristotelian syllogism, nor committing to two premises from which a conclusion is inferred. Truly, the Aristotelian syllogism may be used by one whose habit suits it, not because knowledge of the desired is absolutely dependent on it. So logical syllogism is a 'mental exercise' with no practical benefit. 'And the Sharia of Islam and knowledge of it is not dependent on anything learned from non-Muslims at all, even if it is a valid way.'
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage opens with Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Aristotelian syllogism. He gives an example: a person who recognizes that everything in existence is a sign of God (آية الله) naturally infers that the existence of creation entails the existence of the Creator. This inference uses the relation of ملزوم (the necessary condition, i.e., creation) and لازم (the necessary consequent, i.e., the Creator). Ibn Taymiyya argues that this reasoning does not require casting the argument into the form of an Aristotelian syllogism with two premises. He concedes that some people may use the formal syllogism out of habit, but it is not necessary for knowledge. He likens the formal syllogism to a mere mental exercise with no practical benefit. Crucially, he asserts that Islamic law and its knowledge are not dependent on anything learned from non-Muslims, even if that thing is valid. Thus, the logical syllogism is not epistemologically essential.

Key Terms
الملزوم
اللازم
القياس المنطقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Ibn Taymiyya consider the Aristotelian syllogism to be?

B) A mental exercise with no practical benefit
C) The foundation of Islamic knowledge
D) A necessary condition for any proof

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that formal syllogism is not necessary for knowledge; inference through entailment (ملزوم–لازم) suffices, and Islamic knowledge is independent of Greek logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Evaluate the claim that the Aristotelian syllogism is a 'mental exercise' by testing whether a simple categorical syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., all intoxicants are forbidden, nabidh is intoxicant, so nabidh is forbidden) can be re-expressed as an entailment argument. Which is more natural?
